Preface
Bo Sommer Nielsen, s130344 and Henrik Tang Lauridsen, s170329 are the authors of 
this master thesis, which represent 15 ECTS points in the education Master in Fire 
Safety during the period September 2018 to March 2019 at the Department of Civil 
Engineering at DTU. 
Supervisors from DTU were Associate Professor Luisa Giuliani and Associate Professor 
Frank Markert. 
Date: 1 March 2019
Bo Sommer Nielsen, s130344
Henrik Tang Lauridsen, s170329
==PAGE:4==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
4
Abstract
Current designs of well-ventilated car parks are based on the assumption that a local 
fire will involve no more than 3-4 cars at the same time. Several past and recent car 
park fires, such as the major car park fire in Liverpool in 2017, where more than 1000 
cars were destroyed by fire in a semi-open, multi-storey car park building, have 
proved this assumption wrong and highlighted the need for further research with the 
aim of defining more reliable design fires for car parks. 
In order to do so, the increased amounts of plastic materials used in modern cars 
have been investigated. Furthermore, the effects of conventional fuels, such as 
gasoline, and alternative fuels, such as hydrogen, used in modern cars have also been 
considered. 
This study aims at developing a numerical CFD car model capable of simulating fire 
spread from one vehicle to another. The CFD car model will include a conventional fuel 
(gasoline) and an alternative fuel (hydrogen gas). 
The CFD car model has been developed using Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS), version 
6, and Pyrosim 2018.
The numerical model is based on five submodels – each representing a group of 
combustible parts in a modern car or a specific fuel. The purpose of the submodels is 
to determine the fuel load of a modern car and to give an estimate on the HRR for the 
fuel used in each submodel. 
Selected data from the FDS submodels have been incorporated into a final car model, 
which comes in two versions – depending on the type of fuel considered (gasoline or 
hydrogen). 
The study indicated that the total energy released from the numerical car model is 
approximately 6.9 GJ. This is in good agreement with the experimental data found in 
the CTICM Guideline and experimental studies by INERIS including full-scale fire tests 
of modern ICE vehicles.
The numerical car model’s capability to simulate fire spread to another vehicle has 
been investigated. The FDS simulation indicated fire spread from one car model to 
another after approximately 9 minutes. This is roughly 3 minutes earlier than the 
assumption in the CTICM guideline, which states that fire spread from one vehicle to 
another happens after 12 minutes.    
1.1.1
Keywords
FDS Modelling, Fire Modelling, Car Fire, Hydrogen Fuel Vehicles,
Polymers, Solid Phase Pyrolysis, Gasoline Spill Fire, Hydrogen Gas Release

FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS

1
Introduction and hypothesis
1.1 Background and Motivation
Over the years, several car park fires have occurred resulting in damaged cars and extensive 
damage to building structures. On New Year's Evening 2017 at the Echo Arena situated on 
Liverpool’s waterfront, a Land Rover burst into flames resulting in an 8-level open-sided car 
park fire destroying around 1.150 vehicles [1]. The fire engulfed seven floors of the multi-story 
car park and the heat produced were hot enough to melt aluminum and cause severe damage 
to the concrete structure of the building. According to a British newspaper the fire lead to the 
evacuation of 4,000 people during the fire and the damage costs reaching approx. £ 20 million 
[2].
A guideline valid since 2002 conducted by the CTICM (Centre Technique Industriel de la 
Construction Métallique) in France on the fire safety in car parks assume a fire spread rate to 
adjacent vehicles every 12 minutes, a vehicle reference peak HRR of 8.3 MW and a maximum 
of 3-4 vehicles on fire at the same time [3]. In the car park fire at Echo Arena in Liverpool, 30 
cars were reported on fire at the same time. This could indicate that car park fires are more 
severe than guideline predictions can account for and that the assumptions made in the CTICM 
report back in 2002 can be questioned.  
The car park fire in Liverpool has highlighted the need for further research on the fire spread 
within cars and between cars to achieve more reliable design guidelines for open and semi-
open car parks. To do so, the changes in the material distribution of modern cars, where the 
amount of combustible plastic has increased, must be addressed and investigated. 
Furthermore, the effects of the fuels used in modern cars, both conventional (gasoline) and 
alternative fuels, such as hydrogen gas, should be addressed and considered.   
The fuel load of modern cars is higher compared to older generation cars, because more 
lightweight materials are integrated in the design and manufacturing of cars. For instance, a 
report by Ridge National Laboratory [4] has pointed out that the use of plastic materials in cars 
has increased by 40 % between 1995 and 2014. This development has been driven not only by 
motives to achieve a vehicle weight reduction to increase fuel efficiency, but also an increased 
focus on sustainability and ambitions to improve the car manufacturers’ green brand.   
The increased fuel loads of new cars are likely to have an effect on not only the fire spread 
within the car, but also on the fire spread between cars in a car park fire scenario.
1.2 Object and Aim
The aim of the project is to develop a numerical CFD model capable of simulating fire spread 
from one car to another. The effects of different fuels will also be considered and the CFD 
model will include both gasoline, a conventional fossil fuel, and hydrogen, an alternative 
carbon-neutral fuel. 
1.3 Method
The software used for developing the numerical CFD car model is the Fire Dynamics Simulator, 
(FDS), version 6, developed by National Institute of Standards (NIST) and VTT Technical 
Research Centre of Finland. The applications Smokeview 6.4.4 and Pyrosim 2018.2.0730 have 
been used with FDS during the writing of the thesis.
==PAGE:9==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
9
The numerical car model is based on five submodels:
1) A submodel of the tires.
2) A submodel of the seats. 
3) A submodel of the cabin interior.
4) A submodel of a gasoline spill.
5) A submodel of a hydrogen gas release.
Each submodel is modelled on the basis of a case study, which includes experimental data 
from relevant articles and observations from full scale car fire tests.
The overall purpose of the submodels is to determine the heating value of a modern car and to 
give an estimate on the HRR for the fuel used in each submodel. 
In FDS, the fire spread in submodel 2 and 3 (seats and cabin interior) is based on solid phase 
pyrolysis, whereas submodel 1, 4 and 5 are based on gas phase combustion.
Sensitivity analyses and checks on the amount of energy released in the submodels are 
performed and discussed. Furthermore, some of the submodels are validated against relevant 
experimental data found in literature.
Selected data from the submodel simulations have been incorporated into a final car model, 
which comes in two versions: 
1) A conventional car, which features a gasoline spill fire and interior solid phase pyrolysis.  
2) A hydrogen fuel cell vehicle, which features a thermally activated hydrogen gas release. 
The results from the simulation on the conventional car model are discussed and compared to 
experimental data from full-scale car fire tests. 
This includes: 
 - A comparison of the total energy released from the final model with the experimental data 
from car fire tests conducted by CTICM and INERIS 
 - A comparison of the temperature measured in the final model compared to recorded cabin 
temperatures from full-scale car fire tests. 
Furthermore, the final model’s capability to simulate fire spread from one vehicle to another is 
also considered. The final model (conventional version) has been used to simulate the fire 
spread from one car model to another. 
2
State of the Art and Practice
The development within car manufacturing these years is very progressive regarding 
development of new technology and design. This section will focus on some of the main drivers 
in this development, which also contain fire relevant issues.
==PAGE:10==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
10
2.1 Modern Cars
Modern cars contain many different synthetic materials and metals. With reference to a recent 
article from the U.S. Department of Energy's Office of Energy Efficiency and Renewable Energy 
(EERE), the table below presents the average distribution of combustible and non-combustible 
materials in light weight1 cars in the US between 1995 and 2014 [5]. 
The general tendency of the development is that the total weight of light vehicles has 
increased by only 8% since 1995, although the use of combustible materials has increased by 
28 %, see Table 1 below. 
Another interesting information from the same article is that the use of light- and high strength 
steel and aluminium in the same period has increased concurrently, while the use of regular 
steel and iron casting has decreased by 15 % and 45 %, see Table 1 and Figure 1 below.

this development have not been investigated further, as corresponding data for 2019 US light-
weight cars have not yet been found. 
Looking at the data from 2014 from EERE [5] it shows, that around 8,2 % of the total mass of 
a 2014 lightweight car in the U.S is plastic or plastic composites.

materials in today's automotive industry booms these years. Furthermore, the study states 
that the ongoing development is driven by a constant drive to build lighter, safer and more 
economical cars. Also, the question of preserving fundamental resources is mentioned as one 
of the reasons to the development.

2.1.2
Polymers
The development of modern cars involves an increased usage of lightweight materials in most 
cars according to a recent study by Patil, A et al. [7], which states that:
The main aspect in choosing the high-performance plastic materials in relation to other 
materials used in automobiles are the design of automobiles, their functionality and more 
economic manufacture, as well as reduced fuel consumption. It is estimated that every 10% 
reduction in vehicle weight results in a 5% to 7% reduction in fuel usage.
Describing some of the advantages of the increased usage of polymers, Patil, A. et al [7] in 
their study highlights some important reasons why the usage of polymers has seen such a 
huge growth:

Minimal corrosion, allowing for longer vehicle life

Substantial design freedom, allowing advanced creativity and innovation 

Flexibility in integrating components 

Safety, comfort and economy 

Recyclability
Regarding the amount of polymers, the research by Patil, A. et al. [7] states that the average 
amount of polymers in cars 2006 is 150 kg of plastic, which corresponds to approx. 10-15 % 
of the total weight of a modern car. In Figure 3 below the development in usage of polymer in 
cars from 1970 to 2006 is shown.
Figure 3 Development in usage of polymers in car manufacturing, reproduced from Patil, A. et al. [7]

Furthermore, the same study highlights a list of components often made by plastic and which



In this thesis the material properties of polypropylene and polyurethane will be used in the 
numerical model described in section 6. Polypropylene will used to represent the interior of the 
car cabin, whereas polyurethane will represent the seats. 
2.1.3
Hydrogen Fuel Cell Vehicles (HFCVs) 
This paragraph contains a short description a hydrogen fuel cell vehicle (HFCV) and how a 
hydrogen fuel cell works. Furthermore, the most widespread on-board hydrogen storage 
system is described.
A simplified illustration of a powertrain in a hydrogen cell vehicle is shown in figure 4. The 
hydrogen vehicle is essentially an electric vehicle, because it is powered by an electric motor, 
where a hydrogen fuel cell stack converts hydrogen into electricity that powers the motor [8]. 
The fuel cell stack device consists of many individual fuel cells that operate in a series circuit to 
produce the electric current needed to run the vehicle. 
The electric motor requires high voltage, typically 200-500 volts and 200-300 amps. The high 
voltage lines connect the fuel cell stack to the electric motor, which is located in the front of 
the vehicle. The battery in the vehicle allows for power storage to start the vehicle and to 
compensate for variations in the fuel cell voltage output.
 
Figure 4 Schematic overview of the drivetrain of a hydrogen vehicle. Adapted from figure 1 in Issue 49: Fire Safety for 
Hydrogen Fuel Cell Vehicles [8]  
2.1.4
Fuel Cell Technology 
The most common fuel cell technology applied in hydrogen vehicles is based on Polymer 
Electrolyte Membrane (PEM) fuel cells. The PEM fuel cell consists of an electrolyte membrane 
sandwiched between an anode (negative electrode) and a cathode (positive electrode). 
==PAGE:14==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
14
The PEM electrolyte membrane, which is a thin solid, organic compound, with a thickness of 2-
7 sheets of paper, functions as an electrolyte capable of conducting charged ions (H+), but not 
electrons (e-). 
When hydrogen is channeled to the anode, the hydrogen’s negatively charged electrons are 
separated from the positively charged protons. This process is referred to as catalysis.
The PEM membrane allows the positively charged protons to pass through to the cathode, but 
not the negatively charged electrons.  As a result, the negatively charged electrons must flow 
around the membrane through an external circuit. This flow of electrons forms an electric 
current. At the cathode the diverted, negatively charged electrons (e-) and the positively 
charged hydrogen ions (H+) combine with oxygen to form water (H2O) and heat. Compared to 
other fuel cell technologies, PEM fuel cells require less time to power up and operate at lower 
temperatures. This makes them suitable for vehicle applications.
Figure 5 Schematic overview of a PEM fuel cell in operation. Adapted from 
http://www.fuelcelltoday.com/technologies/pafc.   
2.1.5
On-Board Hydrogen Storage Systems 
Hydrogen vehicles typically employ a high-pressure gaseous storage system to provide the 
hydrogen for the fuel cell stack. The compressed hydrogen is stored in a carbon fiber-
reinforced cylinder at a storage pressure of 700 bar (70 Mpa). The tank capacity is typically in 
the range of 4-5 kg, which corresponds to the amount necessary for the HFCVs to achieve 
their desired running distance of 400-500 km (travelling autonomy).
Although other technologies are being developed, such as cryo-compressed hydrogen storage 
[9], the above hydrogen storage system is the most widespread and the only current 
technology capable of providing an acceptable balance of vehicle range, storage volume and 
system costs.
==PAGE:15==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
15
Figure 6 Photo of a high-pressure hydrogen storage tank installed in a Toyota Mirai HFCV vehicle. 
[https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Toyota_Mirai_hydrogen_tank_and_electric_battery_SAO_2016_9030.jpg]. 
2.2 Investigation of Car Fire Tests
2.2.1
Presentation of Experimental Studies on Car Fires
In this section, a review of selected experimental studies on car fires is done. During the 
review, a list of key information will be extracted from the studies, because these are relevant 
to the final FDS model in this thesis.
The review of the experimental studies will focus on:  
•
Ventilation conditions
•
Ignition method
•
Fuel load
•
Heat release rate (HRR)
•
Total energy released (MJ)
2.2.2
VTT: "Experimental study of fires in a passenger cars"
Some of the first experimental studies on fire in passenger cars were conducted at VTT 
Building Technology in Finland in the early 1990s by Mangs and Keski-Rahkonen [10] . The 
studies included full-scale fire tests of three cars manufactured in the late 1970s equipped with 
oil, petrol and passenger cabin materials under an indoor hood. The intention of the test was 
to describe the burning behavior and to determine the heat release rate of a car placed in an 
open car park. The tested cars were small-size passenger cars with a pre-fire weight of 990 kg, 
918 kg and 1102 kg, which, compared to modern cars and the final FDS model described in 
the numerical model described in section 6, may be considered small and not fully suitable as 
a reference regarding car size in the FDS model in the thesis. 
Describing the experiment from VTT is important in relation to the final FDS model, because 
the tested cars at VTT contained 30 liters of gasoline each and were ignited with 1,5 liters of 
heptane in an open tray under the left front seat. During fire tests, thermocouples and 
radiometers distributed inside and outside the car measured relevant temperatures and heat 
flux during the burning of the cars. 
==PAGE:16==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
16
Ventilation conditions of the tested cars were slightly different in the three tests, but in 
general, at least one window was fully open and either one door or three side windows were 
opened 5 centimeters. These ventilation conditions will be used as reference in the FDS mode 
in the thesis. 
The RHR curve in Figure 7 shows a peak heat release rate just 2000 kW, which occurs between 
12 and 27 minutes for both Test 2 and 3. Considering the size of the tested cars a peak HRR of 
2 MW and a total energy release of 3,0-3,9 GJ also indicate that the amounts of combustible 
material in 1970 cars were not significant compared to modern cars. This is underlined by the 
fact that approximately one third of the total energy release comes from the 30 liters of 
gasoline (44,2MJ/kg x 30 kg = 1,3 GJ).

2.2.3
CTICM: "HRR reference curve for car fire in closed car park"
In 1995 another campaign involving experimental fire test of 18 cars was conducted at the 
CTICM (Centre Technique Industriel de la Construction Métallique) by a group of European 
partners from France, Luxemburg, Spain, Belgium and The Netherlands. 
The result of the campaign was presented in the technical steel research report: "Development 
of design rules for steel structures subjected to natural fires in closed car parks" described by 
Schleich et al. [11]. The studies focused on investigating the heat release rate from full-scale 
fire tests of cars manufactured in both the 1980s and 1990s. As in the tests by Mangs et al 
[10] the test cars were equipped with four tires, oil, spare tire and 40 liters of fuel. Ignition 
was done by igniting 1,5 liter of petrol in a tray under the left front seat.
The report from CTICM indicates that the energy release from a 1995th car is significantly 
higher than from a 1980th car. Looking at Figure 8 the CTICM fire test showed a peak HRR of 
8,3 MW. 
The total energy released from family car was 6800 MJ.
Below a reproduction of the reference curve by CTICM is shown:

With reference to the final FDS model, the reference curve from CTICM will be used to 
benchmark the HRR and total energy released from the final FDS model.
2.2.4
INERIS: "Test of Electric- and Internal Combustion Engine Vehicles"
During the last decade (2010s) an increased focus on more energy efficient cars has been 
highly prioritized, both in society and particularly within the car industry, where many 
manufacturers compete in developing new power trains that are both affordable, energy 
efficient and safe.
In the light of some of the challenges with the new car types, a recent study by INERIS in 
2012 have carried out full-scale fire tests of four modern cars. Two of the cars were ICE-
vehicles (Internal Combustion Engine vehicles) and two cars were EV-vehicles (Electrical-
vehicles). The main purpose of the test was to investigate the overall behavior of EV-vehicles 
in case of external stress and contact with water by fire intervention. The results and the 
evaluation of the experimental fire tests are described in the report: "Comparison of the fire 
==PAGE:18==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
18
consequences of an electric vehicle and an internal combustion engine vehicles" submitted by 
Lecocq, A. et al. [12].
During fire tests ignition was caused by a 6-kW gas burner subjected to the left front seat and 
windows in the cars were open during tests.
Both ICE vehicles contained gasoline in the fire tests. The tank of ICE vehicle 1 was 60% full, 
leaving around 28 L gasoline in the tank, while ICE vehicle 2 was 100% full, equal to 60 L. 
Information on the amount of gasoline in the tested ICE vehicles has been given by Lecocq, 
Armandine from INERIS, see 9.1.1Appendix KAppendix K.
In the table below key information retrieved from the fire tests by Lecocq, A. et al. [12] is 
listed.

2.2.5
Other Experimental- or Numerical Studies on Car Fires
In 2015 experimental studies were followed up by numerical investigations on smoke gasses 
and temperatures. The car fire test took place at the Federal Institute for Material Research 
and Testing in Berlin. The research was conducted by Krüger, S et al. [13]. 
Relevant to this thesis is, that the study, after performing a full-scale fire test, included a FDS 
model, which could be used to investigate the correlation between the measured and the 
modelled temperatures inside the passenger cabin. 
Relevant to the final FDS model in this thesis is that the grid in the FDS model by Krüger was 5 
centimeters and that the breakage of glass was set to happen at 570°C, as windows at this 
temperature in the experimental fire test started melting. The car had no gasoline in the tank. 
The FDS model in the study involved the material properties of only two combustible materials. 
The materials were polypropylene and polyurethane, which represented the upholstered car 
seats.
In addition, the research highlighted that many automobile products, such as upholstered 
seats, side panels, flooring and door coverings exposed to radiation of 25 kW/m² and an 
additional ignition source will ignite within 120 seconds.
In the FDS model by Krüger et al. [13] the materials used as fuel is described below.

information on validation of non-charring polymers.

2.2.6
Fuel Tanks in Car Fires 
This chapter contains an investigation of how gasoline fuel tanks are involved in car fires. 
Some of the test observations have been used to determine the gasoline fuel fire scenario for 
the gasoline submodel in FDS. 
Over the years, several full-scale car fire tests have been conducted. However, the tests rigs, 
the type of cars, the year of manufacturing, the point of ignition and the amount of fuel in the 
tank of the tested cars vary from one test to another resulting in varying observations and 
results. Nevertheless, some common characteristics can be found as to the burning 
characteristics of the fuel and how the fuel tank releases its fuel content.
VTT Building Technology, Finland [15]
The full-scale experiments at VTT Building Technology in Finland by Mangs & Keski-Rakhonen 
[15] also included observations on fuel tanks in car fires. During the research, three full-scale 
fire experiments on passenger cars manufactured in the late 1970s were carried out. The 
amount of fuel in the tanks were 30 L. The cars used in the tests had steel fuel tanks as 
standard equipment. For test car no. 1, the fuel hose to the engine and the filler pipe were 
==PAGE:21==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
21
made of steel, whereas test car 2 and 3 featured plastic fuel hoses and plastic filler pipes. The 
connection of the engine fuel hose to the fuel tank showed two different designs: For test car 
no. 1 and 3, the connection to the fuel tank was placed at the top of the tank. For test car no. 
2, the connection to the fuel tank was situated at the bottom of the tank. The latter design 
variations of the fuel tank connections resulted in different fuel burning observations during 
the experiments, where the fuel in the tanks burned at different modes. This can be seen from 
the HRR curves, where the ignition of the fuel is represented by a distinct peak on the curve. 
See figure 11.
For test 1, the ignition of the fuel occurred after approximately 29 minutes. For test 2 and 3, 
fuel ignition occurred after 20 minutes and 24 minutes respectively. During test 1 and 3, the 
gasoline burned slowly from the tank filler pipe: In the case of test 1, it burned from the 
mouth of the filler pipe. In the case of test 3, it burned from the mouth of the fuel tank, where 
the plastic filler pipe had melted away. The burning duration of the gasoline were 
approximately 1 hour and 20 minutes.
In test 2, gasoline leaked out through the fuel hose opening at bottom of the tank after 
melting of the plastic fuel hose. As a result, the gasoline burned both as a pool/spill 
underneath the car and from the mouth of the tank itself, where the plastic filler pipe had 
melted. Here, the gasoline burned out in approximately 15 minutes. Post fire inspection of the 
cars showed that all of the steel fuel tanks were without damages.
Figure 11 HRR curve for test 2. The red arrow indicates the time when fuel starts leaking from the tank. 
The immediate ignition and burning of the fuel is followed by a peak in the HRR. Adapted from figure 3 
in Characterization of the Fire Behaviour of a Burning Passenger Car. Part 1: Car Fire Experiments by J. 
Mangs et al. [10]. 
Fire Test Report by Channel Tunnel Safety Unit, Department of Transport, UK [16]
Another full-scale test study was performed by the Fire Research Station under the Building 
Research Establishment in Watford, UK [M Shipp & M Spearpoint]. The aim of the experiment 
was to measure the ‘worst case’ fire behavior parameters of motor cars under well ventilated 
conditions. The test included two middle range cars manufactured in the middle 80s. The first 
test car, a Seat Maestro, was manufactured with a steel tank and a steel fuel tank filler. The 
amount of fuel in the tank were 40 L. The car was ignited in the passenger compartment from 
a slashed cut in the driver’s seat with a no. 7 crib fire starter (a fire starter based on small 
==PAGE:22==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
22
wood sticks). After 13 minutes of burning there was a notable increase in the fire size, which 
appeared to be due to a spill of gasoline. Approximately 4 minutes after the observation of the 
fuel leakage, the intensity of the fire was so severe seriously affecting the test rig and 
damaging parts of the measurement equipment. Consequently, for safety reasons, the fire was 
extinguished, and the test was terminated.
Post fire inspection of the car showed that the rubber hose connecting the fuel filler pipe to the 
fuel tank had been damaged by the fire in the passenger cabin. This was believed to have 
allowed gasoline to spill directly from the tank onto the ground underneath the car causing the 
intense fire during the latter part of the test. [M. Shipp and M. Spearpoint].
BD 2552: BRE Report on Full Scale Car Fire Tests [17]
During 2007 to 2009 the BRE (Building Research Establishment) Group conducted a series of 
car fire tests. The research was commissioned by the Department for Communities and Local 
Government in the UK. 
The aim of the test report was to gain knowledge on the nature of fires involving the current 
design of cars and to use this knowledge to assess the need for updating current guidelines on 
fire safety strategies for car parks. The fire tests included single car fire tests, tests on stacked 
cars and investigations on fire spread between cars by radiant heat. Most of the cars tested 
were manufactured in the early 2000s. Test 7 involved a 2002 Ford Focus with standard 
equipment and 20 liters of fuel in the tank. The material of the fuel tank was not mentioned in 
the report, but it is assumed to be made of plastic, since the report refers to that 85 % of new 
cars in Europe have plastic tanks. The point of ignition was the engine compartment.
Test observations reported of a spillage of fuel after approximately 40 minutes of burning time. 
The spill and the burning of the gasoline is associated with the 4.8 MW peak on the HRR chart 
below.
Figure 12 HRR curve for test 7 in the BRE fire test programe. The ignition and burning of the fuel spillage is followed 
by a sudden peak in the HRR. The peak occurs after approximately 40 minutes. Representation of figure 2.15.2 in 
the report [BRE].
Fire Tests with Sedan Passenger Cars [18]
In 2007 the National Research Institute of Police Science in Japan [K. Okamoto et al.] conducted 
a series of fire tests involving sedan passenger cars. The aim of the experiments was to 
investigate the effects of burning behavior if ignition happened in the rear part of the car 
==PAGE:23==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
23
instead of not in the engine compartment or the passenger cabin. In the case of an ignition at 
the rear end of the car, the distance to the fuel tank and the mouth of the fuel filler pipe would 
be shorter. Consequently, it was expected that the gasoline would be ignited at an earlier 
stage of the fire causing a more rapid fire growth. 
The cars in the experiments were used 4-door sedan passenger cars manufactured in the early 
1990s. The cars were equipped with 4 tires and a spare tire. The fuel tanks were made of steel 
and the amount of gasoline in the tanks were reported to be 10 liters (test car A, B and D) or 
20 liters (test car C).  For test car A,B and C the point of ignition was at the splash guard of 
the right rear wheel. The ignition source was alcohol gel fuel.  Test car D was ignited with 2 
liters of gasoline spilled around the left front seat and an electric heater. [K. Okamoto et al.].
In test A,B and C the incipient flame of the splashguard spread to the rear bumber, which  
caused ignition of fuel vapors within 20 minutes. As for test car A, a flame emerged from the 
tank from the mouth of the filler pipe after 21 minutes followed by larger flames emerging at 
the bottom of the car at the rear end on the right. Test car B exhibited a similar burning 
pattern with gasoline vapors igniting around the right rear wheel after 16 minutes followed by 
an emerging flame at the mouth of the filler pipe after 19 minutes followed by larger flames 
forming at the right rear wheel after 22 minutes. The burning progress for test car C showed 
emerging flame at the mouth of the filler pipe after 10 minutes followed by ignition of gasoline 
around the right rear wheel. After 13 minutes gasoline leaked and a large gasoline flame was 
formed at the rear end of the car. During the tests it was observed that in experiment C, the 
flame formed around the right rear wheel was larger compared to test A and B. This was 
believed to be related to the larger amount of gasoline in test car C (20 L), which resulted in 
more gasoline vapor leaking from the tank, when it was heated. The test also showed that the 
larger quantity of fuel caused a faster spread of fire into the passenger cabin. This led to the 
notion that a larger amount of gasoline in the tank is associated with a larger gasoline flame 
formed at the rear and also a faster spread of flame into the passenger compartment.
In experiment D, where ignition occurred inside the passenger cabin, the fire spread to the fuel 
tank after 48 minutes followed by immediate ignition and flames forming around the right rear 
wheel. Approximately 30 seconds later gasoline was observed leaking from the fuel tank. [K. 
Okamoto et al.].
==PAGE:24==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
24
Figure 13 HRR curve for experiment D. The black arrow marks the beginning of the fuel spill (fuel 
leakage) followed by immediate ignition and burning. The subsequent burning of gasoline can be seen 
as a distinct 3 MW peak on the HRR curve. Representation of figure 8 in the test report [K. Okamoto et 
al.].

Based on the above test observations, the following assumptions can be made on the burning 
behavior of the fuel tank and the fuel:
a
Spill fires are likely to occur due to fuel tank leakages, which are caused by the melting of 
fuel hoses, rubber joints or plastic fuel filler pipes connecting the fuel tank.
b
The point of ignition affects the time until the fuel of the tank is involved in the fire: If the 
car is ignited at the rear, the time until the fuel is involved in the fire is shorter compared 
to an ignition location at the engine compartment. In the BRE fire test, where the test car 
was ignited at the engine compartment, the ignition of the fuel occurred after 
approximately 40 minutes. On the other hand, when ignition occurred at the splashguard 
at the right rear end of the test car, the time to fuel ignition was between 12-23 minutes.
c
The burning behavior of cars changes with the amount of fuel in the tank: A larger 
quantity of gasoline in the tank will cause larger flames and presumably also a faster fire 
spread into the passenger compartment.
d
The ignition and burning of a fuel spillage is often indicated by a distinct jump on the HRR 
curve. The flow rate of the fuel leakage will affect the burning duration of the fuel. 
However, fuel (vapors) burning directly from the mouth of the filler pipe will also affect the 
filler pipe.

2.2.7
The Burning behavior of Car Tires
In 2011, the United States Department of Justice, Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and 
Explosives conducted a series of tests on the burning behavior of unmounted car tires [19]. 
The aim of the tests was to provide a basic understanding of the behavior of car tires when 
exposed to flame. In relation to this thesis the review of the experimental fire test of car tires 
serve as important background knowledge on what HRR and heat flux to expect from a burning 
car tire.
Tire Components
Typical tires consist of a tubeless radial-ply construction characterized by rubber compounds 
and embedded cord plies arranged at 90 degrees to the direction of travel. 
The materials found in tires include, steel and polyester chords, synthetic compounds, carbon, 
oil, various additives, fiberglass and cotton [19]. The different surfaces of the tire are referred 
to as the tread, sidewall, bead and liner/well. The tire rolls on the tread, which also contains 
the embedded chords. The liner/well refers to the inner surface of the tubeless tire. The size of 
the tires used in the test were standard 195/60R15 indicating a tread width of 195 mm, a 
sidewall height to width aspect ratio of 60 % and a 15 inch (380 mm) nominal rim size.      
Test Methods
Nineteen ignition attempts were carried out. Ignition methods included non-accelerated 
methods (lighter and propane torch), while others were accelerated by a standard ignition 
package (SIP) involving gauze and paper towels together with 250 ml gasoline in a plastic bag 
The points of ignition included the four main surfaces of the tire: Tread, sidewall, bead and 
well/liner.
Relevant to the final FDS model are the tests with tires in on-tread position, where the test 
layout is shown below:
  
Figure 14 Tire test layout. Red squares indicate the position of the heat flux gages. Representation based on figure 2 in 
the Burning Characteristics of Automotive Tires [18]
==PAGE:27==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
27
Test Results
Burning Duration
Eleven ignition tests resulted in a sustained ignition leading to a full fire involvement of the 
tire. The other ignition tests resulted in self-extinguishment. Eight of the eleven successful tire 
burnings provided usable data regarding the burning duration, which includes the time span of 
the incipient flame development to the decay stages of the fire.   
Table 7 Burning duration of the tires in On-tread position [min]. Adapted from table 1 in the Burning Characteristics of

As for the on-tread oriented tires the tests showed a single primary HRR between 350 kW and 
450 kW with a burning period between 10 and 15 minutes. In relation to the final FDS model 
the HRR curve for the accelerated ignition (SIP Well) will be used as reference, because this 
curve represents the HRR and has a duration over approx. 20 minutes.

Summary of Tire Tests
Ignition location and the use of accelerants are important parameters, which significantly affect 
the overall fire size and burning duration: Depending on the point of ignition, the test results 
showed that the fire duration ranged between 16.5 min to 47.5 min. 
The spread in burning duration is related to the time needed for the incipient flame to develop 
into actual fire growth. This delay was significantly longer for ignition at the tread by 
accelerant compared to ignition at the well or bead regardless of the use of accelerant. 
The on-tread oriented tires burned between 10 min to 15 min with peak heat release rates 
ranging from 350 kW to 450 kW and were characterized by a single peak burning rate. 
Measured at a distance of 0.61 m and a height of 0.46 m, the on-tread orientated tire showed 
peak heat fluxes in the range of 20 kW/m2 to 35 kW/m2. 
The study claims that high heat fluxes from fire in an on-tread orientation tire could potentially 
ignite another tire at the same distance (0,61 m). However, the required minimum exposure 
period for this to occur has not been determined in the test report cf. Gratkowski, M. [19].

Listing some of the key information derived from the listed car fire test it is clear, that "older" 
cars manufactured in the period from 1970-1990 contained less energy compared to the total 
car mass.
Also, the information shows that both the size of cars and the total energy released (Heat of 
combustion [GJ]) have grown significantly since the test at VTT in the early 1990s.
Summing up it seems like that cars in general have become bigger and that the fraction of 
combustible materials in the cars also have increased. Some of this development presumably 
is done to compensate for the increased weight and to improve the fuel efficiency of modern 
cars. 
3
Theory
In the following, a short description of the basic theory behind FDS is presented along with 
some of the limitations of FDS.
3.1 Computational Fluid Dynamics Modelling
The CFD (computational fluid dynamics) modeling technique is used in a wide range of 
engineering disciplines. The modelling is based on a complete, time-dependent, three 
dimensional numerical solution of the fundamental conservation laws [20].
The basic idea of CFD modelling is based on a computation domain, which is divided into a 
large number of cells (sub-volumes) and to numerically solve a set of controlling (differential) 
equations describing the conservation of mass, momentum and energy, combined with sub-
models using different solution algorithm. The submodels include turbulence (flow) and 
radiation. Within each cell, the properties are assumed to be uniform. Mass, momentum and 
energy transport between cells are directly solved using the controlling equations. Within each 
cell, besides the controlling equations, sub-models are also used.
Thermodynamic flows, or turbulence, can be described by the so-called Reynolds number. All 
flows are stable below a certain Reynolds number. Stable flows are also referred to as laminar. 
However, when the flows exceed a certain Reynolds number, the flows become unstable and 
turbulent. Between these two regimes (the stable and the turbulent) the flows are referred to 
as transitional flows. Turbulence sub-models are generally classified into three types, Navier-
Stokes Models, large eddy simulation (LES) and direct numerical simulation (DNS).
==PAGE:30==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
30
Each FDS sub-model have their strong and weak points depending on the type of combustion 
and turbulence flow simulated. However, some of the turbulence sub-models require a very 
fine grid and small time steps resulting in very long computational times.   
Many CFD programs exist today. However, FDS (Fire Dynamics Simulator), which has been 
developed by NIST, is the most widely used for fire-engineering purposes. 
FDS is a CFD software tool based on fire-driven fluid flow. The results of an FDS simulation can 
be displayed using the Smokeview application, which is the companion visualization program 
for FDS.      
For the simulations in the report, the default settings in FDS have been used. 
The default settings include:
1) Hydrodynamic Model: The smoke and heat transport in the models are solved numerically 
on the basis of a form of the Navier-Stokes equations appropriate for low-speed (less than 
Mach 0.3), thermally driven flow. Turbulence in the simulations is treated by means of LES 
sub-model (Large Eddy Simulation). 
2) Combustion Model: The simulations in the report are based on a “simple chemistry” 
combustion model, which is characterized by a single step, mixing-controlled chemical reaction 
using three lumped species of air, fuel and products (H2O, CO2 and CO). Information about 
the reaction is provided on the &REAC line. The reaction between fuel and oxygen, happens 
infinitely fast and it is controlled only by mixing, hence the name mixing-controlled.
3) Radiation Transport: Radiative heat transfer in the models is based on the solution of a 
radiation transport equation for a gray gas. The equation is solved on the basis of the Finite 
Volume Method (FVM).
4) Boundary Conditions: The domain boundary used in the submodels is modelled with open 
VENTS allowing for an open domain, where ambient conditions exist beyond the domain 
boundaries. 
Furthermore, some of the sub-models involving pyrolysis and also the main models employ 
multiple meshes, which involves different grid sizes within the same domain. This is done to 
reduce the computation time.
3.1.1
Limitations of FDS
• FDS can only solve the transport of heat and mass for low speed flows. This means that 
modelling a gas cloud explosion or a (supersonic) jet flame, both characteristic of a hydrogen 
TPRD gas release scenario, cannot be simulated correctly and approximations will have to 
made.
• CFD modelling of pyrolysis presents many limitations. Pyrolysis is difficult to model and 
simulate fully: Firstly, many of the fuels have complex geometries, where the thickness could 
be incompatible with grid size, thus suggesting that the fuel and the fluid field are at different 
scales. Secondly, pyrolysis is such a complicated phenomenon that the present pyrolysis 
models embedded in the CFD software in many cases do not succeed in simulating pyrolysis 
even for single sample tests [21]. 
==PAGE:31==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
31
• CFD modelling with fine grids, resulting in many subvolumes in the domain, strongly depends 
on the performance of the computer hardware, which can become a bottleneck. Some 
computer hardware is capable of running parallel processing to reduce computation time. 
However, in some cases, this technique may cause instability problems (software crashes) and 
a reduction of the accuracy of the modelling [21]. In general, grid cell size is a key issue 
influencing both accuracy and computation time.    
• Gas phase combustion: Even though a simulation might involve the combustion of different 
fuels with different chemical reactions, FDS by default allows only one reaction to take place. 
Multiple reactions can be set up in FDS. However, simulations based on this principle excludes 
the use of specifying a HRRPUA or a MLRPUA to a VENT [22]. Furthermore, the drawback of 
using multiple reactions is computational cost.
3.1.2
Applications 
The Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) is a computational fluid dynamics model (CFD), that is 
capable of simulation fire-driven fluids [22]. The U.S National Institute of Standards and 
Technology (NIST), together with Technical Research Centre of Finland (VTT), have developed 
FDS.
The applications Smokeview 6.4.4 and Pyrosim 2018.2.0730 are used together with FDS6 
during the writing of the thesis.
3.2 Analytic Models
Before creating the FDS submodels, a basic validation of FDS as simulation tool was done by 
benchmarking an earlier study by Søgård, D. [23], who also investigated modelling of car fires 
in FDS.
Documentation on the benchmark process can be found in 9.1.1Appendix A.
3.3 Fire Spread
Fire spread is an important aspect when assessing the fire safety of a car park. In many fire 
scenarios, the fire spread between vehicles determines the duration of the fire and the hazards 
involved for people trying to evacuate. Furthermore, the extent of the fire spread often 
determines the possibilities for the fire and rescue service to fight the fire and to carry out 
other emergency service actions.
Fire spread can be seen as sequence of ignition processes and it comprises five different types 
of mechanisms [21]:
a
Flame impingement, that is, fire spread due to flames touching an object directly. Flame 
impingement is related to the term “piloted ignition,” with a distinction between a) a pilot 
flame impinging directly on the surface, where heating occurs by convection and/or 
radiation and b) a pilot flame or spark that ignites fuel vapors near a surface, but without 
heating the surface. 
b
Flame spread across a surface.
c
Remote ignition or “flash over,” that is, ignition by radiation and sometimes through 
convection.
==PAGE:32==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
32
d
Fuel transfer, that, is fire spread by burning liquids (often fuels) or by burning debris, 
sometimes also referred to as “fire brands.”
e
Explosions causing the spread of fuel and fire.
3.3.1
Fire Spread over Solids
Fire spread over solid materials is closely related to the theory of ignition and it includes the 
following types of ignition [21]:
a
Ignition of fuels vapors released from the surface of the solid.
b
Smoldering ignition, that is, a low temperature, self-heating, flameless form of 
combustion.
c
Direct ignition of the surface of a solid material, such as the ignition of a metal in a fire.
d
Ignition by a chemical reaction occurring directly inside the solid, such as in explosives. 
First mentioned ignition type is the most common type for fire spread over solids. The process 
of producing fuel vapors is also referred to as pyrolysis. Pyrolysis is characterized by a 
chemical decomposition of the solid material, where large molecules are broken down to 
smaller molecule fragments that can escape the surface of the solid as gases and then burn in 
the flame [21], [24]. The released gases are also referred to as “volatiles” implying that the 
molecule fragments undergo several changes during the burning process. 
For ignition to occur several preconditions must be met: 
a
The material needs to heat sufficiently to produce fuel vapors in concentrations suitable for 
ignition.
b
The fuel vapors need to be mixed with an oxidizer (usually air) to form a flammable 
mixture.
c
The temperature of the fuel/air mixture needs to be increased to ignite (auto-ignition) or, 
alternatively, an external heat source must be applied to ignite the mixture, such as a 
spark or a flame. The latter type of ignition is also referred to as forced or piloted ignition. 
Figure 17 Simplified schematics of the processes involved in ignition of a solid material. Adapted from figure 11.1 in 
Tunnel Fire Dynamics by Ingason et al. [21]
==PAGE:33==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
33
3.3.2
Ignition of Solids
The below figure reproduced from Drysdale [24] shows the phases involved in spontaneous 
ignition of a solid material, like for example a polymer used in car manufacturing. 
Figure 18. Ignition of solids – schematic figure of main phases, reproduced form Drysdale [24]
3.3.3
Thermal Decomposition of Polymers
In modern cars many several types and composition polymers are used according to recent 
studies by Patil et. al [7]. The burning behaviour of these polymers include thermal 
decomposition, also described as pyrolysis, where gaseous fuels caused by high temperatures 
at the surface release polymer molecules. According to Drysdale, D. [24] the required surface 
temperature of a burning solid to pyrolyze is typically 400 °C.
3.3.4
Pool and Spill Fires
Several factors determine the rate at which a flame will spread across a pool of liquid fuel. The 
fuel temperature and the fuel depth are key parameters determining the rate of the flame 
spread.
The difference between a pool and a spill should be pointed out: Pools are characterized by a 
confined body of fuel that typically has a depth greater than 5 mm [25]. Pools typically occur, 
when a liquid fuel collects at a low spot on the ground or as a result of the way the liquid is 
stored, such as in an open container. On the other hand, fuel spills are associated with an 
unconfined body of fuel, characterized by thin layers of fuel of 2-3 mm or less depending on 
the substrate [21], [25]. 
Both pool fires and spill fires are likely to occur in a car park fire. However, full scale car fire 
tests [10], [17], [18] have only reported continuously flowing gasoline spills resulting from 
fuel tank leakages. For this reason, spill fires are considered the most likely of the two and for 
this reason spill fires will be the point of focus.       
3.3.5
Spill Fires
Spill fires will occur following a release of a liquid fuel in a location, where there is no 
horizontal confinement. The size and burning rate of a spill fire is highly variable depending on 
==PAGE:34==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
34
the way the flammable liquid is released, the nature of the surface (for example, asphalt or 
ground), the time of ignition and the depth of the spill.
Fuel spills can be characterized as continuously flowing or static, where the fuel spill is 
generated by a momentary release of the liquid. The two types also differ as to when the spill 
is ignited: 
For a static spill, the liquid is assumed to spread to its maximum area before ignition and 
subsequently flame spread will occur across the surface.    
In the case of the continuously flowing spill, ignition typically occurs while the fuel is moving 
away from the source. For a continuously flowing spill fire, the flames may spread across the 
fuel surface initially, but flame propagation is ultimately controlled by the rate at which the fuel 
can spread over the substrate until steady-state burning conditions occur. When steady-state 
burning occurs, the spill size will be controlled by a dynamic equilibrium between the 
volumetric flow rate and the volumetric burning rate of the liquid fuel.  In the event of a 
continuously flowing spill that is not ignited, the spill surface area will continue to increase until 
a physical boundary is met or the fuel source is depleted [25].
If steady-state burning is assumed, a steady-state burning area Ass of the spill can be 
determined from: 𝐴𝑠𝑠 [m] =  
𝜌 𝑉𝐿 
𝑚"
Where VL [m3/s] is the volumetric flow rate, the fuel density ρ [m3] and the mass burning rate 
ṁ” [kg/s] [21].
The nature of the surface also affects the spill size and the mass burning rate. Substrate 
features like porosity of material and surface roughness will result in different spill sizes and 
fuel burning rates. For instance, on a smooth surface the liquid will tend to spread faster 
compared to a rough surface, which may impede the spreading and consequently the size and 
burning rate of the pool.
The depth of a spill will depend on how well the liquid can flow away from the source under the 
influence of gravity. On a smooth horizontal surface, where little impediment is expected, the 
depth will primarily be determined by the viscosity of the liquid, that is, the surface tension of 
the liquid, which is temperature specific. If the surface characteristics of the substrate is 
rough, deeper spill depths can occur, because the fuel cannot spread to its full potential.
On smooth surfaces, the average depth variation of many hydrocarbon fuel spills is very little. 
This is because many of the fuels have similar surface tension. For this reason, the surface 
topography becomes the most important variable, when considering fuel spill depths, because 
rough surfaces could, result in deeper spills characterized by longer burning durations and 
higher peak mass burning rates [24], [25].
However, if the spill considered involves steady state burning, the depth of the fuel spill can be 
disregarded, because the fuel is continuously supplied and the spill is not confined [25].
To determine the HRR from spill fires with low rate releases (< 10 L/min) Ingason et al. [21]   
recommend that one fifth of the pool burning rates should be used. 
==PAGE:35==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
35
Similarly, in line with the above assumption, Ingason et al. have pointed out that the HRR per 
fuel surface area can be reduced by 70-80 % if the fuel spill depth is only a few millimeters 
deep [21].   
The following empirical equation, based on obtained data from liquid pool fire tests, can be 
used to determine the HRR [MW/m2] cf. Ingason et al., page 402 [26]:

To calculate the HRR from an unconfined fuel spill with a low rate release, where the fuel layer 
is expected to be only a few millimeters deep, the mass burning rate value for pool fires must 
be reduced by 1/5 of the original table value.
3.3.6
Heat of Combustion
All combustion reactions take places with the release of energy [Drysdale]. The heat of 
combustion ∆Hc , or heating value, refers to the amount of heat released during complete 
combustion with oxygen under standard conditions (25 °C and at atmospheric pressure). 
There are two kinds of heat of combustion – higher heating value (HHV) and lower heating 
value (LHV) – depending on whether the water is in the form of liquid or vapor. 
LHV calculations assume that the water component of the combustion process is in vapor state 
at the end of combustion, whereas HHV calculations assume that all of the water in a 
combustion process is in a liquid state at the end of combustion. The difference between the 
LHV and HHV is the latent heat of evaporation of water. 
LHV calculations are useful, because they take into account that the energy required to 
vaporize the water in a combustion process is temporarily lost and not released as heat that 
can contribute to fire spread. The heat of combustion is often expressed in the units [KJ/g] or 
[MJ/kg].
4
Case study
As described in section 2.2 several studies performed since the early 1990s until 2012 
highlight that the total mass loss during in a car fire can vary from 176-275 kg. Within this 
span lies a huge development in both technical and manufacturing materials. 
To be able to conduct a FDS model that represents a simplified version of modern car, setting 
up some sub models with the most common combustible materials is necessary. By doing this, 
it is possible to model some of main contributors of combustible objects in isolated domains 
and hereby insure that material properties and HRR fully correspond. Furthermore, the sub 
models make it possible to evaluate the ignition process but also measure the amount of fuel 
represented by the isolated fuel. 
==PAGE:36==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
36
Finally, the sub models have the great advantage that simulation can be performed in smaller 
domains, which allows to run several simulations with different mesh sizes.
The following sub models have been developed:
•
Tires (Rubber)
•
Seats (Polyurethane foam and Polypropylene fabric)
•
Interior car components (Polypropylene)
•
Gasoline Spill Fire
•
Hydrogen Gas Dispersion
4.1 Submodel 1 – Tires
Even the rapid development within car manufacturing modern cars still use rubber tires. This 
submodel therefore includes a FDS model of an ordinary On-tread car tire, which is modelled 
with reference to section 2.2.7.
4.1.1

Assumptions for material properties of the modelled tires has been found in SFPE Handbook 
[25], Engineering Toolbox [27] and recent experimental study by Gratkowski, M. [19].

4.2 Submodel 2 – Seats
From experimental studies by Krüger et al. [13] it is clarified, that the temperature inside a car 
cabin is highly influenced by the material properties of the interior car parts. In this section a 
simplified model of car seats will be presented. 
The development of the submodel has been set up to increase the knowledge on FDS as a tool 
to simulate interior fire spread in a car. The sub model has been created on the basis of an 
example from the FDS User Guide [22] section 11.5.2, where modelling of upholstered 
==PAGE:40==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
40
furnishing is described briefly and with inspiration from Thunderhead Tutorial Guide, "Room 
Fire" [28].
4.2.1
Material Properties
The modelled seats consist of two polymer materials, which have been defined with inspiration 
from a recent study by Krüger et al. [13] which included both experimental test and numerical 
modelling of a car fire. The material represents the inner cushion and the outer fabric of the 
seats.

FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS

Figure 20 Image of FDS Submodel Seats – ignitors placed on the right front seat
4.2.3
The Burning Behaviour of Seats
As described above, car seats empirically add a significant contribution to car fire. This 
behaviour is due to the fact that seats is made of foam with a low density and further an outer 
fabric a durable textile, also with a low density. The combination of low densities and the 
material properties creates a very flammable object, that burn very quickly.
4.2.4
Heat Release Rate (HRR)
A reference curve for burning of car seats has not been found in the literature during this 
study. In addition, the investigated experimental tests also do not give a clear view on how 
much heat burning car seats contribute to a car fire. An early estimate of the expected HRR is 
therefore estimated on the assumption that car seats to some extent correspond to Light-
weight type A upholstered furniture as described in table 3.6 Enclosure Fire Dynamics written 
by Quintiere, J. et. al [20]. The HRR for this furniture is 1700 kW/m². Converting this value to 
a car cabin, where the total area can be estimated to be 2-3 m², the resulting HRR potentially 
could be within the range of 3400-5100kW.
4.2.5
Domain and Boundary Conditions
The domain is 2mx2mx2m and modelled with OPEN Boundaries OPEN. Mesh sizes are 
described in Table 17.
4.2.6
Ignition
Ignition sources is modelled as Particles in FDS with a surface temperature of 1000°C. Place-
ment of the particles are 50 mm from the cushion and the back rest of the front passenger 
seat. Two ignitor particles are modelled to insure sufficient temperature close to the seat to 
onset the pyrolysis and later as pilot for the ignition.
Placement of the particles have been chosen with reference to where the experimental fire 
tests ignited the test cars.
==PAGE:42==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
42
4.2.7
Verification of Material Properties
To ensure that the material input parameters for the seats submodel are valid, a basic check of 
the energy output against the theoretical fuel load is done. In FDS this is done by "burning" a 
simple foam block in a domain with open Vents. The Burner is a 1200°C hot surface placed 
under the foam block. The foam block is modelled with "Seats" properties.
Figure 21 Capture of 0,2x0,2x0,2m Foam block in 0,6x0,6x0,6m open domain

approx. 1700 kW/m².
The area of the foam block is approximately 0,04 m². The expected HRR for the one seat, thus

The convergence is relatively poor even and the energy amount for the smaller grids seems to 
stabilize around at around 75 % of the theoretical energy amount. 
As for this relatively big deviation a clear explanation has not been found, other than the 
material properties are not precise enough. Also the fact that modeling pyrolysis in FDS include 
some limitations and uncertainties, claimed by Ingasson, H. et al. [21], may also reduce the 
credibility of the foam block submodel.
The simulations of the seats submodel are simulated in both Mesh 05 and Mesh 10, because 
the finer mesh does not add better results.

4.3 Submodel 3 – Interior 
Construction of modern cars involve many different polymers depending on the location and 
function. This is due to the low weight and durable properties, but also manufacturing cost and 
recycling abilities according to to recent studies by Patil, A et al. [7] and Hovorun, T. P. et al. 
[6].
In this section one of the most used polymers in car manufacturing is investigated – the 
chosen polymer is: Polypropylene. 
4.3.1
Material Properties
Polypropylene has the polymer unit:
 
Figure 24 Extracted from wikipedia, https://da.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polypropylen
The basic information on material properties has been found in "An Introduction to fire 
dynamics" by Drysdale [24] and a recent study by Krüger et al. [13] which included both 
experimental test and numerical modelling of a car fire.

As the seats sub model, the interior components in the PP Submodel can be categorized as 
thermoplastics that burn through solid phase pyrolysis.  Following the same procedure used for 
the seats the reaction in FDS is modelled with a complex reaction that includes pyrolysis and 
"burn away" of the combustible material.

The amount of PP fuel can be estimated to: 0,15 m³ x 910 kg/m² = 136,5 kg. Knowing that 
manufacturing of a modern car includes many other polymers than PP, the amount of fuel

and size.
Figure 25 Image of FDS Sub model with interior components – Ignitor (hot surface, 1200°C) is placed under to insure 
onset of pyrolysis and ignition.
4.3.3
Burning Behaviour of Interior
The interior polymers parts in a modern car in this submodel consist only of polypropylene, 
which is a well-known thermoplastic material.
The burning behaviour of polypropylene is through solid phase pyrolysis and release of 
combustible gasses, that ignite if the temperature increase above the ignition temperature 
(approx. 334°C) [24].
4.3.4
Heat Release Rate (HRR)
The expected HRR for the interior before running any simulations is assumed to lay within the 
range of 2000-3000 kW. The basis for this assumption is a full-scale test described by BRE 
[29], where 117 kg polypropylene / wool carpets reach 2,4 MW in an un-sprinklered 
environment.
4.3.5
Domain and Boundary Conditions
The domain is 2mx2mx2m with a concrete slap underneath and modelled with all other 
Boundary Vents OPEN. Mesh sizes are described in Table 23.
4.3.6
Ignition
Ignition sources is modelled as a Heater in FDS with a surface temperature of 1200°C to insure 
complete combustion and to compensate for the lack of convection in the cabin.
==PAGE:47==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
47
4.3.7
Verification of Material Properties
Like the Seats submodel, it is necessary to validate the material properties input parameters 
for the Interior submodel (Polypropylene). Again, a basic check of the energy output against 
the theoretical fuel load is done by "burning" a simple PP block in an open domain. The Burner, 
again, is a hot surface (1200°C) placed below the PP foam block. The foam block is modelled 
with "PP" properties.
Figure 26 Image of PP block from Pyrosim inside a box with holes. Boundaries are OPEN.
4.3.8
Calibration
The calibration is done with the assumption that the interior components represent a heating 
value, that is equal or bigger than the corresponding value for the seats. The same mesh sizes 
therefore is used when modelling the PP sub model, see Table 23 below:

4.1 Submodel 4 – Hydrogen 
The use of hydrogen as a fuel is considered a clean, sustainable technology, with no harmful 
emission gases (such as CO, NOx and SOx) and an advance towards reducing long-term 
releases of greenhouse gas emissions. 
4.1.1
Material Properties
Hydrogen is a colourless, odourless and nontoxic gas. It is the lightest of all gases with a very 
low density per unit volume. Hydrogen is highly flammable over a wide range of temperature 
and concentration. When ignited, the gas burns with a low visibility flame. Hydrogen has the 
highest energy content per unit mass of any fuel. On a weight basis hydrogen has nearly three 
times the energy content of gasoline with 141.7 MJ/kg vs. 46.4 MJ/kg.  However, on a volume 
basis the situation is reversed. For example, the energy content of liquid hydrogen is roughly 
8.490 MJ/m3 as opposed to gasoline with roughly 31.150 MJ pr. m3. [30].  
==PAGE:50==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
50
Table 26. Some selected thermophysical properties for hydrogen.
Heat of Combustion
Fuel
Density [kg/m3]
Higher Heating Value (HHV) 
[MJ/kg]
Lower Heating Value (LHV) 
[MJ/kg]
Hydrogen 
H2
0.090
141.7
120
4.1.2
Pressure Relief Devices
Hydrogen vehicles employ a high-pressure gaseous storage system to provide the hydrogen 
for the fuel cell stack. The High-pressure hydrogen storage cylinders are designed with a 
pressure relief device (PRD) in order to prevent a dangerous rupture of the cylinder in case of 
overheating by fire. Since the relief device is thermally activated, it also called a TPRD 
(Thermally activated Pressure Relief Device). 
When the temperature of the cylinder reaches 110 °C, the TPRD will activate and the 
combustible gas content of the cylinder will be released through a vent pipe underneath the 
car, cf. Tamura et al. [31]. 
4.1.3
Gas Release Profile 
In order to investigate fire spread behaviour in case of a hydrogen dispersion underneath 
HFCV, the time-dependent transport of hydrogen gas via the TPRD during the blow-down of 
the high-pressure cylinder must be considered. 
The cylinder gas release can be described through a so-called release profile, which can be 
used as a boundary condition for a dispersion simulation. Figure 29 below illustrates a release 
profile for a 700 bar high pressure hydrogen gas release of 4 kg. 
After 84 seconds, the release rate drops below 1 g/s. The release rate curve is based on a real 
gas model (Noble Abel equation of state) given that an ideal gas equation will overpredict the 
release rate and the amount of gas released, cf. P. Middha et al. [32]. The initial release rate 
of the TPRD vent is 0.45 kg/s. 
==PAGE:51==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
51
Figure 29. Release profile for a high-pressure hydrogen gas release. Adapted from figure 1 by P. Middha et al. [32]
From the curve it can be seen that roughly 50 % of the total amount of hydrogen gas is released during the first 7 
seconds of the 84 seconds.
4.1.4
Release Scenarios 
The hydrogen gas release during a blow-down can be characterised as a continuous gas 
release with two possible outcomes depending on the time of ignition: Instantaneous (direct) 
ignition results in a jet fire, while delayed ignition results in a flammable gas cloud formation 
underneath the vehicle followed by an explosion.
Anecdotal accounts by various research and testing organizations have pointed out that fires 
that cause TPRDs to open, may not immediately ignite the vented gas. The fire that triggers 
the thermally activated TPRD is often not in a position that causes the vented gas to ignite, 
because the exit from the vent may not be situated in the fire. For this reason, delayed ignition 
as the flammable cloud builds and expands to an ignition source is an acceptable assumption 
and a likely scenario, cf. Venetsanosa et al. [33]. 
Figure 30. Event tree of a continuous release blow-down from a high-pressure hydrogen gas cylinder.
==PAGE:52==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
52
4.1.5
Hydrogen Release Scenario Modelled in FDS
Modelling a supersonic jet flame or a gas cloud explosion in FDS is not possible, because the 
algorithms of the FDS model is based on low-speed Mach numbers of less than 0.3. 
Consequently, the FDS software cannot simulate flow speeds approaching the speed of sound, 
such as explosions, jet flames and supersonic flows at nozzles. 
Because of the limitations of FDS, the gas cloud formation and subsequent burning underneath 
the car has been approximated by simulating a 3 m2 surface area capable of releasing 
hydrogen gas with a variable mass flow rate based on the release profile of figure 29. 
Given the limitations of FDS, the principle of releasing hydrogen gas over an area underneath 
the car is considered a reasonable approximation to a gas cloud forming and burning 
underneath the car.  
The amount of hydrogen gas introduced during the simulation is 4 kg, which is the resulting 
hydrogen mass quantity you get when the area underneath the curve in figure 29 is estimated. 
The following FDS input parameters have been used in the submodel:
1) The surface area of 3 m2, representing the area of the gas cloud underneath the car, is 
modelled using the &VENT parameter.
2) The hydrogen gas has been defined by &SPEC name list group in FDS, which is used to 
define gas species. Once the correct species ID has been provided, in this case hydrogen, the 
physical properties of the species are pre-defined and do not need to be specified. During 
simulation FDS will use precompiled data to compute the various thermophysical properties of 
the gas, some of which are subject to change as the temperature changes in the domain. 
3) The release of the gas is introduced via parameter MASS_FLUX(1). The initial mass flux rate 
is 0.15 kg/(m2∙s). With a surface area of 3 m2, the initial mass flow rate of 0.45 kg/s from the 
release profile is matched. The mass flow rate of the hydrogen gas release is controlled by the 
RAMP_MF(1). The parameter MASS_FILE=.TRUE. instructs FDS to produce an output file 
containing a time history of the hydrogen mass introduced through the 3 m2 VENT surface on 
the concrete surface cf. figure 31. In accordance with the release profile, a time span of 84 
seconds of gas release has been simulated. 
4) In the main model, the simulation of the TPRD releasing hydrogen gas, when the 
temperature near the on-board gas cylinder reaches 110 °C, is modelled using the &DEVC 
parameter.
==PAGE:53==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
53
Figure 31 FDS Smokeview screendump of the hydrogen sub-model. The hydrogen gas is introduced through the 3 m2 
VENT (yellow surface area) on a concrete surface. The domain is 560 m3.
Figure 32 The recorded time history of the hydrogen gas mass introduced through the 3 m2 surface area in the FDS 
submodel. The graph is generated from the output data csv.-file in FDS.
4.1.6
Sensitivity Analysis
Sensitivity studies have been carried out in the hydrogen sub-model.
1) The combustion efficiency of the hydrogen gas has been investigated in the model by:
a) Varying the domain volume.
b) Varying the amount of hydrogen gas released into a fixed volume domain.  
2) The effects of the mesh size in relation to:
a ) The recorded amount of gas released through the 3 m2 VENT.
==PAGE:54==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
54
b) Total energy released by hydrogen combustion.
1a) Varying the volume of the domain
The simulations were based on a fixed gas release of 4 kg. The mesh size in all the domains 
were 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.10 m. A simulation with the standard domain of 72 m3 (6 m x 4 m x 3 m) 
and a 4 kg hydrogen gas release resulted in the lowest combustion efficiency with a total heat 
released of roughly 315 MJ and a 34 % deviation from the calculated (LHV based) reference 
value of 480 MJ. As the volume of the domain is increased the combustion efficiency of the 
hydrogen gas is also increased, probably because more air (oxygen) is available in the domain. 
Increasing the air volume allows for greater air entrainment resulting in an increased amount 
of a flammable air/gas mixture in the domain. The analysis indicates that a domain of 560 m3 
(10 m x 8 m x 7 m) will have the highest combustion efficiency with a deviation from the LHV-
based reference value of roughly 5 %.

1b) Varying Gas Amounts
Varying the amount of gas released into a domain is another way of investigating the 
hydrogen/ air ratio in the model and the combustion efficiency of the introduced gas.
The simulations were based on varying the release of gas by 1, 2, 3 and 4 kg. The volume of 
the domain was fixed at 560 m3. The size of the mesh was 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.10 m.
The analysis points to that if the amount of gas is reduced, the combustion efficiency of the 
hydrogen gas is increased. This might be related to the pressure boundary condition of the 
open vents of the domain, which is most stable for flows that are either mostly in or mostly out 
of the domain cf. FDS User’s Guide, chapter 10.3.2 [22]. As the amount of hydrogen gas 
introduced into the domain is increased, the air flow speed (turbulence from the combustion) is 
also increased. This causes greater pressure differences over the open boundary vents and 
greater deviations on the model output as the amount of hydrogen gas is increased.

2a.  Check on the amount of gas released through the 3 m2 VENT
A sensitivity study on the accuracy of the recorded amount (4 kg) of hydrogen gas released 
into the model has been performed. The simulation was carried out by varying the number of 
cells covering the 3 m2 VENT in the model and the overall number of cells in the domain. The 
volume of the domain was fixed at 72 m3.
The results show that as the number of cells over the VENT increase, a more accurate measure 
can be obtained in FDS. This is expected as the accuracy of the computed mass transport in 
FDS increases with a finer grid. However, the differences of the recorded quantities are 
marginal. For instance, a quadruplication of cells covering the VENT, from 300 to 1200 cells, 
will only increase the accuracy by 6 grams of hydrogen gas.

2b) Total energy released by hydrogen combustion.
A sensitivity study on different mesh sizes and the effects of the total energy released in the 
model has been performed. The volume of the domain was 560 m3. The nominal quantity of 
gas was 4 kg.
The simulations show that as the number of grid cells are increased, the calculated LHV-based 
reference value of 480 MJ is approximated. With a total number of cells of 560.000, the total 
energy released in the model is estimated at 454 MJ, which is approximately 5 % lower than 
the LHV-based reference value of 480 MJ. 
If the number of cells is increased by another 120.000 cells, the deviation from the reference 
value is approximately 4 %.

The sensitivity analysis shows that if the domain volume and the number of cells in the model 
are increased, the reference value of 480 MJ is approximated, and good agreement can be 
achieved.  
With a domain size og 560 m3 and a mesh size of 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.10, the estimated value of 
454 MJ is roughly 6 % lower than the reference value of 480 MJ. The simulation with a finer 
grid of 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.0824 m gives a closer approximation on the reference value with an 
energy output of 460 MJ and a deviation of roughly 4 %. However, the drawback of using a 
domain with 680.000 cells is a very long computational time and, in some cases the finer grid 
resulted in simulation crashes due to ‘numerical instability’ in FDS. For this reason, the 
selected mesh of 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.10 m (560.000 cells), with only a 1.4 % difference in the 
total energy output between the two models, is considered an acceptable approximation to the 
reference value of 480 MJ. 
Consequently, the data generated from the sub-model with a volume of 560 m3 and a grid size 
of 0.10 x 0.10 x 0.10 m will be used in the final car model (hydrogen version).   
4.2 Submodel 5 – Gasoline
Gasoline is a colorless petroleum derived flammable liquid used mainly as a fuel in internal 
combustion engines. Gasoline belongs to a group of organic fuels also referred to as 
hydrocarbons, because the fuels on a molecular level consist of hydrogen and carbon atoms.
 Gasoline is enhanced with a variety of additives, some of which resist igniting too early to 
improve engine performance. Other additives improve the chemical stability of the fuel and 
provide fuel system cleaning. Gasoline contains benzene, which is harmful to the environment 
and a known carcinogen. The combustion of gasoline is considered a major contributor to 
harmful emission gases and global carbon dioxide emissions.
4.2.1
Material Properties
Table 31 Some selected thermophysical properties for gasoline and heptane.

4.2.2
Spill Fire Scenario 
With reference to the observations done on fuel tanks in car fire tests, cf. table 6, the gasoline 
submodel will be modelled as an unconfined gasoline spill fire caused by a leaking fuel tank. 
Total amount of fuel is 40 L - as in the car fire tests conducted by the CTICM [3].
Fuel type: Gasoline
Scenario type: Continuously fed, unconfined fuel spill. 
Flow rate: 40 L/ 25 min = 1.6 L/min = 0,0266 L/s = 2,66 x 10-5  m3/s  
The spill is assumed to be ignited immediately upon the release of the liquid. The burning area 
of the spill will grow in size until an equilibrium spill area has settled. This is referred to as “the 
steady state burning spill area” or “equilibrium spill fire burning area.” 
When this condition is met, the maximum spill area of the fire is known and the fuel involved 
in the fire is continuously supplied. 
For unconfined fuel spills <10L/min one fifth (1/5) of the mass burning rate for pool fires must 
be applied according to Ingason et al. [21], [26]  
→ 0.055 x 1/5 = 0.011 kg/m2/s

4.2.3
Spill Fire Scenario Modelled in FDS
The following FDS input parameters have been used in the submodel:
1) Gasoline does not exist as predefined fuel used. Instead, n-heptane, a hydrocarbon fuel 
with similar thermophysical properties has been used in the gas phase combustion via the 
parameter &REAC FUEL = 'N-HEPTANE.
2) The spill is represented by a 1.8 m2 VENT placed on a concrete surface in a 6 m x 4 m x 3 
m domain. 
3) The mesh size is 0.10 m x 0.10 m x 0.10 m resulting in a total number of 72.000 cells in 
the domain.  
4) The effect spill fire is defined by the parameter MLRPUA defining the mass loss rate of 0.011 
kg m-2 s-1 for the unconfined spill fire. 
5) The burning duration of the simulation is set to the estimated 1505 seconds from the spill 
fire scenario and controlled by the RAMP_Q parameter.
==PAGE:62==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
62
6)  In the final model, the ignition of the spill is initiated, when the temperature near the gas 
tank reaches 160 °C. This modelled using the &DEVC parameter.  At 160 °C a fuel hose failure 
in the fuel tank is assumed.
Figure 37. FDS Smokeview screen dump of the gasoline sub-model. The burning area of the gasoline spill fire is 
represented by a 1.8 m2 VENT (red surface area) on a concrete surface. The domain is 72 m3.
