Check on the HRR in the model
The gasoline model has been validated against the following equation based on experimental 
data to calculate the HRR of pool fires, cf. Ingason et al. [26]: 
𝑞" =  𝑚̇ "
∞(1 ‒ 𝑒‒ 𝑘𝛽𝐷)ℵ∙∆𝐻𝑐∙𝐴𝑓 
For gasoline 
 = 43.7 MJ/kg,  
 = 0.055 kg/(m2s) and 
= 2.1 m-1. 
∆Hc
𝑚̇ "
∞
𝑘𝛽
ℵ ≈1
With a pool fire area of 1 m2, the equation yields a HRRPUA of 2.2 MW/m2.
According to Ingason et al. [21] the HRR per fuel surface area for unconfined fuel spills only a 
few millimeters thick or less, the HRR per fuel surface area must be reduced by 70-80 % of the 
large fuel depth value. 
Taking this into account, and with a spill area of 1.8 m2, the expected HRR would then be:
Table 33. Reference HRR based on equation for expected HRR for fuel spills.
Reference HHRPUA 
[MW/m2]
Reference HRR for spill fire area of 1.8 
m2 [MW]
70 % reduction of calculated HRR
0.66
1.19 
80 % reduction of calculated HRR
0.44
0.79 
Calculation example: 80 % of 2.2 MW/m2 = 0.44 MW/m2. For a spill fire with an area of 1.8 
m2, the expected HRR woould then be 1.8 m2 ∙ 0.44 MW/m2 = 0.79 MW ≈ 790 KW
The model HRR is lower than the experimentally based reference value of 0.66 MW/m2 cf. 
Ingason et al., page 400 [26].

FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS

However, the model HRR output of 0.86 MW is in good agreement with the lower reference 
HRR of 0.8 MW for a 1.8 m2 spill fire.

5.1.3
Radiation
Another valuable information from the sub model is that the radiation from an on-tread 
positioned tire reaches 15-25 kW/m², which correspond relative fine with the experimental 
values stated by Gratkowski, M. [19]. 
==PAGE:67==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
67
Figure 43 Radiation level from car tire between 130 seconds and 430 seconds is above 10 kw/m² in some areas.
5.1.4
Discussion 
Both the HRR and radiative heat flux output values are within range of what have been found if 
the reference study by Gratkowski, M. [34]. Knowing that the criteria for ignition for e.g. PE 
and PP according to literature by Drysdale, D [24] is a critical heat flux of 15 kw/m² there is a 
strong indication that a burning car tire is an important factor looking at fire spread from one 
car to another. The Tire FDS Input RAMP will be implemented in the final model for all four 
tires with the addition that the RAMP first will activate when a temperature of 370°C is reached 
at the individual tires. 
I this way the development of both internal and external fire spread in the final FDS model 
may influence when or if the tires burn.

5.2.3
Smokeview
To illustrate ignition and fire spread in car seats a list of images from Smokeview have been 
extracted to demonstrate some of the output data gained through the modelling. The first four 
pictures show the radiation and timeline: 
==PAGE:70==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS

The pictures above show a clear degradation of the car seats and fire spread from the right 
front seat to the other seats in the cabin. Only half of the seats burn, which explain why the 
total energy in the simulation is much lower than the theoretical value.

==PAGE:71==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
71
5.2.4
Discussion
In the Seats Submodel, the primary focus has been set on investigation on fire spread in and 
between car seats after ignition with hot particles. 
Comparing images from Smokeview with the very different HRR curves, it seems that the finer 
Mesh 05 leads to a more "individual" burning process than in Mesh 10, where the eddies from 
the front- and rear seats seems to unite into one plume.
To investigate the influence of the cabin in the final FDS model, two versions of final model are 
modelled. One with materials (solid phase pyrolysis) and another version with Input RAMPs for 
cf. Seats and Interior components. The version with RAMPs will be used in the hydrogen 
version of final model.
Observing the limited fire spread in the open domain, where no convection is present, it is very 
likely that more foam will combust in the closed cabin of the final model. After running the 
simulations on the final model with materials the degree of complete burning of the seats will 
be considered, when comparing with total energy release with the reference curves.

In the Interior Input RAMP a delay of 300 seconds has been incorporated, in order to insure 
that the more rapid fire growth from the burning seats produce enough heat to onset the 
pyrolysis from the interior components made of polypropylene. The time delay is set from the 
assumption that the temperature in the front end of the lower cabin must exceed approx. 
330°C that, according to Drysdale, D. (Table 1.5) [24] is necessary to thermal decomposition 
of polypropylene.

5.3.4
Discussion
Amount of polymers in modern cars have increased and recent studies [7] indicate that around 
10% of a modern cars weight is polymer. Referring to the amount of PP of 136,5 kg used in 
the final FDS model, this should correspond to a total weight of 1365 kg. Compared to the 
development in car size, which at least in DK show a tendency towards larger family cars and 
SUVs the amount of polymer in the final model may be on the lower side compared to many of 
these some of the bigger new cars.

5.4.3
Discussion
With a total number of cells of 560.000, the total energy released in the model is estimated at 
454 MJ, which is approximately 6 % lower than the LHV-based reference value of 480 MJ.  This 
deviation is considered an acceptable estimate on the energy released by the combustion of 4 
kg hydrogen gas.

model cf. figure 55.
5.5 Results from Submodel 5 - Gasoline
5.5.1
HRR
Figure 61 HRR output for the gasoline fuel model. The burning duration is 1505 seconds. The HRR is approximately 
883 kW.
==PAGE:77==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
77
5.5.2
Smokeview
Figure 62 FDS Smokeview screendump of the simulated spill fire. The HRR is constant for the entire burning duration.
5.5.3
Discussion (gasoline submodel)
The gasoline fuel model overpredicts the estimated total energy release from the spill fire 
scenario by roughly 2 %.
The overprediction is due to the fact that n-heptane, which has been used as a surrogate fuel 
in the simulation, has a slightly higher heat of combustion of 44.57 MJ/kg compared to 43.7 
MJ/kg for gasoline.
To compensate for this overprediction additional FDS simulations could be carried out. Further 
simulations should include: 
 a) A shorter burning time in the FDS model in order to reduce the total energy released in the 
model to get a closer match to the reference value of 1294 MJ.
b) Simulations that override the default heat of combustion for n-heptane in FDS replacing it 
with the specicfic heat og combustion for gasoline.
6
Final FDS Car Models
6.1 Conventional Car (Pyrolysis and Gasoline RAMP)
To be able to gather the different submodels into a final model, a simplified car model has 
been modelled in FDS. Considering that a modern car is extremely complex to imitate in FDS, 
the final model is characterized by great simplification regarding to both geometry and 
materials. 
The final car model is modelled with inspiration from a standard family car, regarding size and 
form, but without any precise reference. In the picture below the left side of the car has been 
hidden to enable a detailed look into the cabin. 
==PAGE:78==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
78
 
Figure 63 Final model measure is 4,7 x 1,8 meters with a total height of 1,5 m.
Before describing the final model in more details, it should be mentioned that the overall 
intention with the final model was to deliver realistic conditions for the submodel fires.
The simplified car model has been modelled from a list of materials, which represent some of 
the basic parts of a modern car and the specific materials from each of the different 
submodels.
6.1.1
Material Properties
In Table 43 below, the additional materials used in the final FDS model are listed:
Table 43 Material properties for seats (sources: SFPE Handbook [25], table A.27 ; Engineering Toolbox [27])

6.1.2
Geometry
All components used to model the simplified car are Slap- or Wall Obstructions modelled in the 
relative coarse grid, Mesh 10, which means that all Obstructions has a minimum length, width 
and height of 0,1 m.
Interior screens inside the cabin are modelled as thin Obstructions in Steel. This is done to 
secure that fire in the cabin cannot directly radiate towards the strategic thermocouple 
(DEVC), that controls the time to release of either Gasoline - or Hydrogen underneath the car.
==PAGE:79==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
79
Also, screens have been modelled as flame barrier towards wheel arches to support that fire 
inside the cabin not immediately reach the tires, which burns through a Ramp function when 
370°C is reached on the surface of the tire. 
 
Figure 64 Placement of Thin Obstructions inside the car cabin
To insure enough ventilation in the cabin the left front window is modelled fully open, while all 
the other side windows are modelled with a 10 cm opening in the top. Windows are modelled 
with 70% transparency, which allows us to look through the glass. The same maneuver is 
done with the left side of the car, because otherwise visual contact to the Interior PP 
components would be limited. The transparency of the car side is modelled with 75% 
Transparency. 
The sides and the roof of the car are layered surfaces consisting of Steel – Air (cavity) – Steel. 
This imitates that these components to some extent are hollow and therefore act as insulation 
around the car cabin. The outer parts of the car are modelled as EXPOSED walls – this has 
been done to insure heat radiation between the inside and the outside fires (fire spread).
In the front of the car and around each tire, holes in the car have been modelled to reduce the 
number of Obstructions around the tires and to insure sufficient ventilation conditions around 
both the wheel arches and the engine compartment.
Figure 65 Placement of "Hole" in the front (red)
==PAGE:80==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
80
The FDS code for the Hole in the front tire is shown below:
&HOLE ID='Hole #1', XB=0.2,0.5,0.8,2.2,0.6,0.9/ 
One feature that if differs the Mesh10 version from the Mesh 05 version of the is, that the rear 
window is modelled to fail when a temperature of 600°C is reach at the Glass Break DEVC.
&DEVC ID='Glass Break DEVC', QUANTITY='TEMPERATURE', XYZ=3.7,2.2,1.2, 
SETPOINT=600.0/
Figure 66 Rear window DEVC position
6.1.3
Domain and Boundary Conditions
The domain of the final model is 5m x 3m x 3m in the scenario where the gasoline spill occurs. 
The mesh for the final model is divided into sub grids in the lower 1,6 m, where the cells are 
0,1x0,1x0,1 m. In the upper 1,4 meter the cell sizes are 0,2x0,2x0,2 m.
The FDS code for the Mesh10 grids is:
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-a', IJK=10,16,16, XB=0.0,1.0,0.0,1.6,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-b', IJK=10,14,16, XB=0.0,1.0,1.6,3.0,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-c', IJK=10,16,16, XB=1.0,2.0,0.0,1.6,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-d', IJK=10,14,16, XB=1.0,2.0,1.6,3.0,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-e', IJK=10,16,16, XB=2.0,3.0,0.0,1.6,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-f', IJK=10,14,16, XB=2.0,3.0,1.6,3.0,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-g', IJK=10,16,16, XB=3.0,4.0,0.0,1.6,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-h', IJK=10,14,16, XB=3.0,4.0,1.6,3.0,0.0,1.6/
&MESH ID='Mesh01-a-i', IJK=10,16,16, XB=4.0,5.0,0.0,1.6,0.0,1.6/

==PAGE:81==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
81
Figure 67 Enlarged domain is required to run the simulation with hydrogen release
For the final models the boundary conditions in general are modelled Open, except the floor, 
where a concrete slap is modelled underneath the car.
For the final models a Gauge Heat Flux is modelled for each subgrid on the right-hand side of 
the model to register the radiative surface integral towards a potential adjacent car.
The FDS code for one of the five Gauge Heat flux DEVCs in the conventional car is shown 
below:
&DEVC ID='Gauge Heat Flux - Right rear door SURFACE INTEGRAL', QUANTITY='GAUGE HEAT 
FLUX', STATISTICS='SURFACE INTEGRAL', XB=3.0,4.0,3.0,3.0,0.0,1.6/
 
Figure 68 Imitation of neighbor car 0,6 m away with 1,6 m height
==PAGE:82==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
82
6.1.4
Ignition
In the Final FDS models the fire starts inside the cabin at the right front seat. Source of 
ignition is hot particles, which are modelled to glow at a temperature of 1200°C in 7 minutes 
at a distance of 50 mm front the right front seat cushion and back rest.
A small detail that difference the Mesh05 model from the Mesh 10 is that the first has three 
particles where the last only gets ignited with two particles. The particles are modelled to 
onset the pyrolysis reaction from the front seat. As the temperature increases the pyrolysis 
starts and a spontaneous ignition of the seat occurs leading to fast fire spread inside the cabin.
The FDS code for the particle ignition: 
&PART ID='Ignitor particle',
      STATIC=.TRUE.,
      SURF_ID='Ignitor',
      AGE=420.0/
&SURF ID='Ignitor',
      RGB=255,102,0,
      TMP_FRONT=1200.0,
      GEOMETRY='CYLINDRICAL',
      LENGTH=0.15,
      RADIUS=0.01/
Figure 69 Placement of the two ignition particles (red)
Initiation of the gasoline spill fire is controlled by a temperature device, which starts at 160°C 
(failure of fuel hose connection). The hose connection is assumed to be made of polypropylene 
(PP), which is a material often used in fuel systems cf. Patil et al., table 1, [7]. PP has a melting 
point of roughly 160 °C. 
6.1.5
Reaction 
In the submodels different REAC parameters in FDS were used depending on the fuel was 
investigated in the model. 
In the final model, where only one REAC can be set in FDS, the default Propane REAC is used, 
because this reaction contains both carbon and hydrogen molecules. 
==PAGE:83==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
83
Usage of one REAC fuel in the Final Model is not a problem in relation to the different 
submodels, because FDS automatically adjust the mass loss rates of fuel gasses for the 
different materials, so that the defined heat release rate for each material is reached. 
6.2 Results
The results from the FDS simulations are presented below together with a brief discussion on 
main topics and key information and benchmarking towards relevant experimental studies.

6.2.2
Heat Release Rate (HRR)
The HRR of the conventional car model with materials and gasoline simulated in Mesh 05 and 
Mesh10 is shown below. 
Before investigating the curves, some background information should be highlighted:
•
Mesh05 version is modelled with three ignition particles placed at the right front seat and 
with breakage of right rear window at 600°C.
This is done to underline that even small adjustments in a FDS model of a complicated 
geometry like a simplified car can influence the simulation results quite clearly.
•
Mesh10 version is modelled with only two ignition particles at the right front seat and 
without breakage of windows. Here the "default" setting with left front window full open 
and other three side windows 10 cm open is used.
Figure 80 HRR for Final FDS model in Mesh05 and Mesh10
The peak HRR for the Mesh 05 model with materials and gasoline reach approximately 5800 
kW, while the Mesh 10 version peaks at 4000 kW.
Important observations for the Mesh10 simulation:
a
Fire spread from the front seats to the back seats after 399 seconds (6 min. 40s). Fire 
development indicate a regular Flash-over in the Cabin.
b
The FDS output file: "Final_car_model_Materials_Gaso_ctrl.csv" shows that the FUEL Leak 
DEVC reached its set-point of 160°C at 722 seconds (12 min. 6 s). At this point the HRR 
"jumps" from approx. 3000 kW to approx. 3700 kW.
c

Important observations for the Mesh05 simulation:
d
Almost immediate ignition of the right front seat by the three particles after 8 seconds.

f

Below the experimental HRR curves reproduced from CTICM [3] and INERIS [12] is shown.
Comparing the reference curves with the FDS curves there is not a precise match on the peak 
HRR, although the mesh05 version rise to almost 6 MW. One of the experiences during 
simulation was that the ventilation conditions in the cabin strongly affect the peak HRR. 
Also, the FDS curves variate from the reference curves in the early phase, where the FDS 
model grows relative fast compared to the experimental tests. This could be due to the 
simplified geometry of the model, that allows fire to spread rapidly between the few 
combustible materials, that contain no incombustible obstructions, near or around them to 
slow down the fire development inside the cabin.

The difference between the theoretical energy amount and the out from FDS is 7.533MJ – 
6.932MJ = 601 MJ, corresponding to -8% energy release in the FDS model. This could very 
well be due to not all PP and tires burn in the FDS model. 
Comparing the total energy amount from the final FDS model with the reference curves it is 
clear, that the FDS models in general release energy that correspond relatively well to the 
reference cars, also the modern cars from INERIS.
One of the factors that influence this result is the amount of PP components in Final FDS model 
of 136,5 kg and the mass of the seats incl. fabric 36,9 kg, together adding a total fuel load 
inside the cabin of 173,4 kg. For the ICE 1 vehicle the total mass loss during fire test was 192 
kg.
As described earlier, the amount of polymer in modern cars often are 10-15% of the total 
mass. Assuming, that 173,4 kg represent 15% of a given car mass, the total weight of the car 
should approximate 1156 kg, which is not very different from ICE 1.

 In the Final Model the cabin temperatures were measured at three strategic points, marked 
with red in the figure below.

Temperatures at the Cabin Mid DEVC escalate fiercely after approx. 7 minutes, where 
temperatures rise to +600°C. From time 7 minutes and till time 55 minutes the temperature in 
the cabin range from 600-1000°C. 
Comparing to recent experimental test by Li. D et. al. [35] such high temperatures were also 
registered at measuring point T7 placed behind driver's seat between 1400s and 2200 s, where 
the measurements were stopped in the test.
One important observation in the output data is, that the temperature for the Fuel Leak DEVC 
rises very fast after 12 minutes which triggers the Fuel Leak DEVC activation, when reaching 
160°C in rear bottom of the cabin. In the DEVC output file it can be confirmed that the 
activation temperature is reached after 726 seconds (12 min. 6 s.), when the CTRL value shift 
from -1 to 1.
Another point of interest is to compare the temperature above the right front tire with 
experimental tests. 
The result is that the FDS model temperature above right front tire quite well match the 
temperature, T4 measured in the experimental test by Li. D et. al. [35]. 
For both the FDS and the experimental study temperature ranges from 400-600°C was found, 
although that in the FDS model the thermocouple is fully exposed to fire in the engine 
compartment, which probably was not the case in the experimental test, where a front wing 
presumably separated the engine compartment from the wheel case.
6.2.5
Fire Spread
Related to the overall focus on investigating fires spread between cars, which the recent fire in 
Liverpool also has proved, the final FDS model is also used to indicate what the expected heat 
flux from a modern car is at 0,6 meters from the car. This distance is chosen with the 
assumptions, that standard parking spots in Denmark are 2,5 m wide and with an average car 
width of approx. of 1,8 m, a distance of 0,6 m correspond relatively well to the distance 
between two cars in a Danish car park.
==PAGE:89==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
89
In the final FDS models the heat flux from the burning car towards "neighbor" car is measured 
by modelling of Gauge Heat flux integrals for the three rear subgrids along the right-hand side 
of the car.

The duration of time where the heat flux is above 15 kW is approx. 28 minutes. Comparing 
with the early results from Mangs et al. [10], figure 5, where heat flux from relatively small 
cars were between 10-30 kW/m² at distance 0,58 and 1,0m within the time span 10 minutes 
to approx. 33 minutes the FDS model seems to give good indication on heat flux. An important 
observation here is the difference in the two methods, where VTT used a fixed-point 
radiometer, where the output data from FDS register the heat flux integral "along" the side of 
an imaginary adjacent car. This method is chosen to illustrate that the total radiation from a 
car fire towards another car at distance 0,6 m is relatively high. 
6.2.6
Discussion
Although the total energy released from the conventional model is in line with the CTICM and 
INERIS reference cars, the outline of the HRR curves of the conventional car model (Mesh 10 
and 05) differ from the outline of CTICM and INERIS reference curves. 
==PAGE:90==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
90
An explanation to this is most likely related to the fact the HRR curves are highly dependent on 
the ventilation conditions inside the cabin. If a window breaks, it is likely to be followed by a 
sudden peak in the HRR. Similarly, the initiation of a spill fire from a leaking fuel tank is also 
likely to generate a peak on the HRR curve. The times at which the ventilation conditions 
change due to windows breaking and the time at which a fuel tank starts to leak will inevitably 
vary from one car fire to another. For this reason, an identical match with the outline of one of 
the reference curves from CTICM or INERIS should not be expected.  
The HRR curve of the final car model also illustrates that the modelling of a fire growth phase 
from a real car fire is quite difficult. Firstly, this is related to the fact that the combustible 
components (interior and seats) of the model are geometrically simple. Secondly, the cabin is 
void of non-combustible items that can impede the fire growth. Thirdly, the car model lacks 
the thermal inertia of the many steel and aluminum components in a real car. As a result, once 
ignition is reached, the fire growth phase in the model happens faster and at an earlier stage 
compared to observations from car fire tests.
The HRR curves from the simulations of the conventional car model also illustrate that the 
effect of the ignition source and the mesh size greatly affects the fire growth phase in the 
model. In the simulation with the finest mesh (mesh 05), ignition was initiated with the aid 
three ignition particles, which caused an auto-ignition of the front seat after 17 seconds. In 
contrast to this, the simulation with a coarser mesh (mesh10), where the fire was initiated 
with the aid of two ignition particles, the auto-ignition of the front seats happened after 
approximately 300 seconds.
6.3 Hydrogen Car (RAMP-based)
The geometry of the hydrogen car version is the same as the conventional car model. The 
hydrogen version of the final car model is a simpler version of the conventional car model 
using only timer-controlled RAMPs with assigned HRRs from the submodels. The red VENT 
represents the seats in the cabin and the blue VENT represents the interior. The seats start 
burning at T=0 seconds. The burning of the interior (polypropylene) starts at T=300 seconds.  
After 300 seconds the temperature in the cabin is at least 330 °C, which is the temperature 
when the denser polypropylene is expected to pyrolyze and burn. The burning of the tires is 
modelled according to the same principles as the conventional final car model.  
Figure 86. The hydrogen car model. The model is based on timer-controlled RAMPS in FDS. The red SURFACE (dotted 
red circle) represents the burning the seats in the cabin, whereas the blue SURFACE (dotted green circle) represents 
the burning of the interior (polypropylene). The VENT representing the hydrogen gas cloud formation underneath the 
car becomes visible, when the TPRD is activated through the DEVC parameter. When activated during the simulation, 
it appears as a green area.
==PAGE:91==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
91
6.3.1
Domain and Boundary Conditions
The domain used for the hydrogen car model is 10m x 8m x 7m with a total volume of 560 m3. 
The domain is bigger that the domain used for the simulation of the conventional (gasoline) 
model. This is with reference to the sensitivity analysis done in the hydrogen submodel, which 
indicated  that the sudden increase in the HRR (high HRR peak) caused by the hydrogen gas 
release requires more excess air in the domain to insure a satisfactory combustible gas/air 
ratio in the domain and to minimize pressure differences on the open boundaries of the 
domain, which might affect the output data from the simulation.
6.3.2
Ignition
The simulation of the TPRD release of the hydrogen is controlled by a temperature device, 
which releases the gas at 110°C, see location of the Fuel leak DEVC in Figure 64. It is modelled 
on the same principle as the conventional model. Only the SETPOINT on the &DEVC line is set 
to 110. 
6.3.3
Reaction 
The fuel in the REAC line is propane. 
6.3.4
Smokeview

Figure 88. (Right) The ramp (red color) representing the seats is burning. The ignition starts at T=0.

6.3.7
Discussion
The peak HRR of roughly 47.000 kW recorded in the hydrogen submodel is not reached in the 
final model simulation. A simulation with the same domain size and grid size containing only 
the propane-based hydrogen release shows that the car body affects the HRR and reduces the 
peak at the start of the release. This might be related to the fact that when TPRD is activated, 
the car body divides the flames rising from the burner underneath the car. This is believed to 
reduce the peak HRR cf. figure 91.
The burning of hydrogen gas is a “clean” burning compared to the burning of hydrocarbon 
fuels, because hydrogen is carbon-neutral with no soot yield and low radition. For this reason, 
you could argue that the use of a propane reaction in FDS to simulate the combustion of 
==PAGE:94==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
94
hydrogen is not a good approximation, because propane contains carbon resulting in a higher 
soot formation and consequently a higher level of radiation. However, it has been pointed out 
that in a real fire, the high-pressure hydrogen gas release will cause dust particles from the 
surroundings to be mixed with the hydrogen flames, which will increase the radiation from the 
hydrogen flames. From that point of view, propane might be an acceptable approximation after 
all. 
Figure 94. A simulation, with the same domain size and grid size, with only the propane based TPRD release in the 
domain. The HRR curve shows that the propane based TPRD release has a similar HRR peak to that of the hydrogen 
submodel. For this reason, it is believed that in the final model simulation, the car body impedes the HRR and reduces 
the peak at the start of the release.
The total energy released from the simulation of the final hydrogen car model is lower than the 
estimated total energy release based on the implemented data from the submodel. 
The deviation is roughly 7 %. 
Considering that the simulation of the final model is based on propane as a surrogate fuel, 
which has a different carbon/hydrogen atom ratio than the fuels used in the submodels, a 
deviation of 7 % in the total heat released is considered acceptable and a good approximation 
to the total energy estimate from the submodels.  
7
Investigation of Fire Spread between Two Cars
To investigate fire spread between two vehicles, a simulation on the final (conventional) 
model’s capability to simulate fire spread from one vehicle to another has been performed. 
The simulation includes two conventional car models, which have been placed 60 centimetres 
apart. This distance is slightly shorter compared to the CTICM test [3], where the distance 
were cf. 0,71 and 0,9 meters between the cars.
The surrogate fuel in the &REAC line in the simulation is propane. 
Ignition of the first car is initiated by two ignition particles placed on the right front seat 
following the same principle as the final (conventional) model. 
==PAGE:95==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
95
The blue car is referred to as car no. 1 and the grey car is referred to as car no. 2. 
Car no. 2 has been modelled with closed windows towards car no. 1. The windows facing the 
domain boundary on car no. 2 have been modelled partly open. 
Figure 95. Simulation investigating the fire spread between two car models. The two cars have been placed 60 cm 
apart. Ignition is initiated in the blue car (car no. 1).
7.1.1
Domain and Boundary Conditions
The domain used for the simulation is 5 m x 5.2 m x 3 m. Domain boundaries are “open” as in 
the previous simulations. Mesh size is 0.1 x0.1 x 0.1 m.

7.1.4
Discussion
The simulation indicates that the final model can generate fire spread from one car to another. 
The second car model catches fire approximately 9 minutes after the first car is ignited. 
Ignition of the right rear tire on car no. 2 happens after approximately 13 minutes and 50 
seconds. Both observations are in good agreement with the assumptions in the CTICM 
guideline saying that fire spread from one vehicle to another happens after 12 minutes [3].  
==PAGE:98==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
98
8
Conclusion
Five submodels have been set up to determine the energy load of a modern car and to 
investigate fire spread both within the car and outside the car. 
The submodels have been calibrated and compared to experimental data found in literature. 
Subsequently, the data from the submodel simulations have been implemented into a final 
numerical model, which comes in two versions depending on the type of fuel used in the car 
(gasoline or hydrogen). 
Submodel 1 – Tires 
Simulations indicated that the HRR from experimental data on burning car tires can be 
achieved, although the simplified geometry caused by the grid prevents a good imitation of a 
round car tire. However, to match the experimental HRR curve of approximately 400 kW, it 
was decided that each tire should be RAMP-controlled with an ignition temperature of 370 °C.    
Submodel 2 - Seats
Simulations indicated that the total energy released from the submodel was approximately 25 
% lower than the reference value. Regarding the total energy released from the final car, the 
energy output value from the seats submodel may be on the low side. However, because the 
seats only represent a minor part of the total energy load of the car model, the energy output 
of the seats is still considered an acceptable approximation. The lower energy output from the 
submodel suggests that modelling solid phase pyrolysis in FDS is a complex task with many 
pitfalls and sources of error.        
Submodel 3 -Interior 
Simulations indicated that the total energy released from the interior submodel is in line with 
the reference value, which was based on the total energy released from 136.5 kgs of 
polypropylene. The simulations also indicated that the simple geometry of the interior and the 
unlimited exposure of the interior components inside the car cabin affect the fire spread rate, 
which happened faster and at an earlier stage compared to experimental data.  
Submodel 4 – hydrogen
Simulations indicated that total energy output from the hydrogen gas combustion are 
dependent on not only the mesh size, but also the volume of the domain. Sensitivity checks 
were performed by either extending the domain volume or by running simulations using a 
fixed domain volume introducing varying amounts of hydrogen gas into the domain. The 
sensitivity analysis indicated that by increasing the domain volume, a good approximation to 
the (LHV-based) reference value of 480 MJ can be achieved.
Submodel 5 – Gasoline
Simulations resulted in a slightly higher HRR (883 kW) compared to the calculated HHR for the 
gasoline spill fire (860 kW). Similarly, the total energy released from the fuel model (1323 MJ) 
was greater than the estimated total energy released from the gasoline fuel spill (1294 MJ). 
This is expected to be related to the fact that the surrogate fuel used in the gasoline fuel 
model was n-heptane, which has a slightly higher heat of combustion compared to gasoline.
==PAGE:99==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
99
The final model
The total energy released from the conventional car model (gasoline) is roughly 6.9 GJ. This 
output value is in good agreement with the experimental data found in the CTICM Guideline 
and the INERIS ICE 1 test, where the total energy released from a mid-sized family car have 
been reported to be around 6.8 GJ and 6.9 GJ, respectively. 
Thus, it can be concluded that the total energy released from the final car model is a good 
approximation to the total energy released from the burning of a modern car.    
The temperatures inside the cabin of the final model have been compared to experimental 
studies. Here, the temperatures measured inside the cabin of the conventional model were 
found to be in the range of 600–1000°C. This temperature range is in good agreement with 
experimental data found on cabin temperatures in car fires, which have been reported to be in 
the range of 800–1000°C. 
Fire Spread Between Cars
The simulation showed that the final car model is capable of simulating fire spread to an 
adjacent car model. The second car model caught on fire approximately 9 minutes after the 
ignition of the first car model. Ignition of the right rear tire on car no. 2 happened after 
approximately 13 minutes and 50 seconds. Both times are in good agreement with the 
assumptions in the CTICM guideline, which states that the fire spread from one vehicle to 
another happens after 12 minutes.  
8.1 Further Studies
The results of the project inevitably lead to some new studies that can be made. 
Further studies could include:
• Further studies could be made on finding another surrogate fuel, which can be used in the 
REAC line for the final model simulations in FDS. Whether or not propane is a good 
approximation, when the simulation represents the combustion of polypropylene, polyurethane 
foam, rubber, gasoline and hydrogen needs to be investigated further. Perhaps a fuel with a 
different carbon/hydrogen count would be a better compromise. 
• Further studies on FDS simulations concerning solid phase pyrolysis of polymers is 
recommended, as the total energy released from the two submodels including polymers did 
not both correspond to theoretical reference values.
• Further investigations on the ventilation conditions of the car cabin should be carried out, 
because the simulations indicate that ventilation conditions in the car cabin greatly affect the 
rate and the intensity of the fire spread inside the car. The extent of openings in the car body 
and the windows of the car cabin (closed or partly open windows) play an important role in 
controlling the ventilation conditions of the fire inside the car.   
• A final hydrogen car model has been developed. However, due to time limitations we were 
unable to run simulations on the HFCV’s capability to simulate fire spread from one vehicle to 
another. For this reason, further studies on the differences concerning fire spread between a 
conventional car (gasoline) as opposed to a hydrogen fuelled car is recommended.

Appendix A
Benchmark of simple FDS model
This appendix represents the benchmarking of basic our FDS model towards in earlier study by 
Søggard, D. [23]. 
Dimensions of the Model and Input Data
A room with the dimensions 6 x 4 x 3 meters is modelled. The boundaries of the room, which 
also defines the computational domain, is by default assumed to be a solid boundary 
maintained at ambient temperature (20 °C), that is, the boundaries of the room do not absorb 
any heat. For validation purposes, this is considered a reasonable assumption.    
The model contains an opening of 2 x 2 meters. The opening is placed on the boundary of the 
computational domain with the surface property &VENT with the parameter SURF_ID=’OPEN’. 
Furthermore, a burner with the dimensions 1 x 1x 0.3 meters, has been placed in the room. 
Only the top surface of the burner contributes to the combustion process,whereas the rest of 
the surfaces of the burner have been made inactive (inert) in FDS. The burner has been 
assigned with a HRR pr. unit area (HRRPUA) of 1 m2. The burner in the model is set to gas 
phase combustion. The combustion fuel for the gas phase is propane, which is assigned to the 
burner by the &REAC line in FDS:
&REAC ID='Propane',
      FUEL='REAC_FUEL',
      FORMULA='C3H8',
      SOOT_YIELD=0.01,
      HEAT_OF_COMBUSTION=46460/
Figure 104 Model of the room in FDS.
The reference model has been divided into 6 sub models, which have been validated using the 
following input data:

The mesh sensitivity analysis has been performed using the average smoke layer heights 
between 20-60 seconds plotted against the total number of cells for each mesh. As the number 
of cells increase, the fluctuations of the smoke layer height decrease, thus indicating 
convergence of the recorded output values in the model. The output values of mesh 5 and 6 
indicate convergence with a number of cells at around 160.000. After that point no devaition in 
the output data can be observed. For this reason, the models have been validated on the basis 
of “mesh 5.” The optimal mesh size is marked with a red circle on the above figure.
Validation of Model 1
Model 1 is used to run a basic check on the HRR and the mass loss rate (MLR) to ensure the 
input energy value converges with the ouput energy value. If the criteria is met, then the right 
amount of energy is going into the domain and the model is considered to work properly. In 
the model, the input HRRPUA is 500 KW and the burner area is 1 m2. Furthermore, a check on 
the mass loss rate (MLR) is performed. Here, the output MLR in the model should match the 
numerically calculated MLR.

The plotted output MLR of the model show good convergence with the calculated MLR. 
However, the output HRR of the model is 30-40 % lower than the calculated HRR. This could 
indicate that the numerical calculation cannot account for the factors that influence the HRR in 
the model. Most likely, because the gas phase combustion in model, after all, isn’t ventilation 
controlled. If the opening in the domain boundary was increased, more air would flow into the 
domain, increasing the combustion rate, resulting in a higher output HRR, closer to the 
calculated HRR.

Validation of Model 3
Model 3 is characterized by a fuel controlled fire with a variable HRR given by the expression: 
 
𝐻𝑅𝑅(𝑡) = 𝛼∙𝑡2
As with the two previous models, the output data should correspond to the numerically 
calculated HRR and MLR. The HRRPUA in model 3 is set to 500 kW. The energy realease rate, 
also refered to as the growth rate, is set to “fast” with an α-value of 0.047 kW/s2. 
The time when the fire reaches 500 kW is calculated as:
 
500 kW = 0.047
kW
s2 ∙t2  → t = 103,142 ≈103 s   
Thus, after 103 seconds the fire reaches its steady phase. 
In order to simulate the above fire growth in FDS, a user-defined function has been set up. 
The command RAMP_Q designates a ramp function to use for the surface assigned to the 
burner [36]. When the simulation starts at t=0, the burner activates and then ramps up until it 
reaches a HRRPUA of 500 [kW/m2]. After 103 seconds, the burner remains at 500 kW until the 
end of the simulation, which is set to 120 seconds.
The Q_RAMP function is based on the calculated HRR at a specific time and a fraction of the 
HRR. 
==PAGE:109==

experimental data clarified by Heskestad even the minor percentile difference. 
Validation of Model 5
Validation of the far-field temperatures is done by comparing the temperature at 2,4 m height 
above the floor collected in four evenly distributed measuring points with McCaffrey's 
experimental correlation [37].

concrete. The end wall where the VENT is placed is left out and modelled without thermal 
absorption. Thermal properties for concrete walls below:

Validation of Model 6
The validation of the neutral plane is done by finding the mass flows in and out of the 
enclosure. This is done by using experimental correlations done by [38] and [37]

Summary
In order to refine and improve the knowledge on modelling of modern car fires in FDS, the 
validated FDS model bases on the same fundamental properties as the model used by [23] in 
term of domain, boundary conditions and hear release rates . By doing this the validation are 
parallel to what have earlier been done, and therefore directly be used as a starting point in 
==PAGE:115==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
115
the further development of utilization and understanding of how modern car fires can be 
simulated in FDS.
A few deviations from the methods used by Søgaard, D. was been conducted, where the need 
for better verification was necessary. In general the changes invalidation strategy and 
documentation have not had a significant impact on the model as being invalid or imprecise 
towards credible numerical results. During the validation is has been emphasized that the FDS 
software with precise input data is a powerful tool, that in the coming process will be useful in 
modelling fire in a modern car.
The result of the validation in general shows, that the FDS model corresponds fine to former 
studies and also to well-known analytic formulas conducted by reputable researchers, and 
therefore represent a valid foundation for continuing the development of a car fire model.

Appendix J
Ignition of Liquids 
Fire Spread over liquids
Carpark fires inevitably involves the burning of combustible liquids, such as gasoline, diesel, 
but also alternative fuels such as hydrogen. For this reason, the mechanisms behind the 
ignition and burning of liquid fuels are important aspects to consider in a car park fire. 
Needless to say, the burning of a liquid fuel represents a hazard in itself, but fuel pools and 
fuels spills also pose a risk of fire spread to other vehicles.
Ignition of Liquids
In most cases, the ignition of liquids does not occur in the form of liquid. Instead, the ignition 
occurs above the liquid surface in the form of fuel vapors mixing with the air forming a 
combustible mixture. Combustible liquids are classified according to their flash points, which is 
defined as the lowest temperature at which a flammable mix of vapour and air exists at the 
surface at normal atmospheric temperature [24].
It follows that highly flammable liquids form flammable mixtures above the liquid surface at 
relatively low temperatures, whereas less flammable liquids form flammable vapour/air 
mixtures at higher temperatures.
Different test methods exist for determing the flash point of liquids, such as the closed cup or 
open cup flash point, and the two test methods produce different values. Furthermore, liquids 
are also classified according to their boiling point and fire point, where the latter of the two is 
defined as the lowest temperature at which the ignition leads to sustained burning .
Table 8 Flash points and fire point for some selected liquid fuels, adapted from Ingason et al. table 11.4 [21]
Material
Closed cup flashpoint [°C]
Open cup flash point [°C]
Fire point [°C]
n-nonane
31
37
42
n-heptane
- 4
- 1
2
The flashpoints are used to classify combustible liquids in order to indicate their relative fire 
hazards. Liquids with low flashpoints indicate a risk at ambient temperatures as their vapours 
may be ignited by a spark or a flame. Different classification systems are employed depending 
on the country or region.
Table 9 Example of the DSEAR 2002 classification system with flash point criteria.

Ignition of Liquids
In most cases, the ignition of liquids does not occur in the form of liquid. Instead, the ignition 
occurs above the liquid surface in the form of fuel vapors mixing with the air forming a 
combustible mixture. Combustible liquids are classified according to their flash points, which is 
defined as the lowest temperature at which a flammable mix of vapour and air exists
Flame Spread over Liquids
Several factors determine the rate at which a flame will spread across a pool of liquid fuel. The 
fuel temperature and the fuel depth are key parameters determining the rate of the flame 
spread.
The rate of the flame spread over a liquid surface is highly dependent on the temperature of 
the liquid and whether or not the temperature lies below or above its flash point or fire point, 
cf Drysdale [24]. At ambient temperature, the vapour/air mixture above the surface of a highly 
flammable liquid, such as gasoline, will be above its lower flammability limit (LFL). Under these 
conditions, ignition of the liquid will be followed by the spread a premixed flame through the 
vapour/air mixture. This is also refered to as gas phase controlled flame spread, cf Ingason et 
al. [21] If the liquid is above its fire point, the premixed flame will develop into a diffusion 
flame followed by a steady burning of the liquid. The gas phase controlled flame spread is 
characterised by relatively fast flame propagation. For hydrocarbons, such as gasoline, it is in 
the order of 1 m/s, cf Ingason et al. [21].
On the other hand, if the temperature of the liquid is below its flashpoint, a different 
mechanism control the flame spread. Here, the surface ahead of the flame front must be 
heated to generate a concentration of vapour above the surface to allow flame advancement. 
Consequently, flame spread rates are significantly lower. This type of flame spread is referred 
to as surface tension-driven flow, cf Drysdale [24] or surface tension-induced spread, cf 
Ingason et al. [21]. The driving mechanism behind the flame spread is due to the fact that 
surface tension decreases as temperature is increased: The temperature difference between 
the flame front and the cooler surface ahead of the front creates a net force, which causes hot 
fuel to be expelled from beneath the flame. This movement of hot liquid desplaces the cooler 
surface layer allowing for the advancement of the flame. At the same time, assuming calm 
conditions, the fully developed diffusion flames will create an air flow acting in the opposite 
direction of the flame spread. This is caused by air entrainment into the base of the fully 
developed flames.  
==PAGE:150==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
150
Figure 116. Illustration of flame spread across a combustible liquid. Reproduced from figure 7.2 in An Introduction to 
Fire Dynamics [24].
As already mentioned, the flame spread rate increases when the liquid temperature increases. 
As the temperature increases, three different regions or regimes have been identified Ingason 
et al. [21]:
I) The liquid controlled region (I), where the flame spread occurs by surface tension-induced 
flow and only a slow increase in the flame spread rate with temperature can be observed.
II) The gas phase controlled region (II) characterised by a significant increase in the flame 
spread rate with temperature.
III) The asymptotic gas phase controlled region (III) characterised by a flame spread rate 
constant with the increased temperature. 
The transition from region I to region II is indicated by Tgo, whereas Tgm indicates the transition 
from region II to region III.  An illustration of the relationship between the regions of flame 
spread and the fuel temperature besides the indication of Tgo and Tgm can be seen from Figure 
3. 
The above experimental observations point to the fact that the most important parameters 
determining flame spread are the initial fuel temperature (prior to ignition), T1, the flash point 
of the fuel, Tfl, and the temperature difference between them.
Table 52 Example of transition temperatures for a selected fuel. Reproduced from table 11.5 in Tunnel Fire Dynamics 
Ingason et al. [21].
Fuel
Tfl [°C]
Tgo [°C]
(Tgo - Tfl)

(Tgm – Tfl)

==PAGE:151==
FIRE MODELLING IN FDS: FIRE SPREAD IN MODERN CARS
151
Figure 117. Representation of the regions of flame spread as a function of fuel temperature. Reproduced from figure 
11.3 in Tunnel Fire Dynamics Ingason et al. [21].
Test results have shown that for hydrocarbons, the maximum flame spread rate in region I 
was  0.1 m/s and in region III between 1.20 – 2.0 m/s Ingason et al. [21].
If the depth of the pool or spill is reduced, the flame spread rate will decrease. This is mainly 
due to a reduction of the internal convection currents in the fuel layer that drive the surface 
tension-driven flow. If heat losses to the supporting material are too great, then flame spread 
will not be possible.
